==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the potential of multivariate probabilistic models
based on a compiled regional soil database, to characterize the correlation behavior between
laboratory measured soil parameters, and to provide support in site-specific design decisions.
To do this, the study adopts basic principles of statistical and Bayesian inference, but also
implements state-of-the-art methods and simulation techniques as these are proposed in
(Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), to construct
site-specific multivariate probability distributions, by utilizing real-life soil data, under
Multivariate, Unique, Sparse, Incomplete and potentially Corrupted (MUSIC) characteristics.
The adopted hybridization approach (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019), keeps a balance between
two multivariate models: a regional multivariate distribution, and a site-specific distribution
constructed from sparse site measurements. The Bayesian approach of Ching, K. K. Phoon,
Khan, et al., 2020, is based on an improved regional multivariate model that reduces the
uncertainty when conditioned on site observations. Both models rely solely on data, i.e. they
belong to Machine Learning methods, while they operate under the Bayesian framework,
allowing strict characterization of uncertainties. Simulation results from both models, showed
that it is possible to capture, localise and reduce uncertainties in correlations between design
parameters and soil index properties, even when few and sparse measurements are available
at the site. Both methods provide updated marginal distributions that allow site-specific
predictions, while updated marginal statistics, computed from a large number of simulated
soil samples, indicated that most coefficients of variation (CoVs), fall within recommended
ranges of published guidelines.

1 Introduction
Reliability methods in geotechnical design are gradually gaining more attention among the
geotechnical community over the recent years. Design standards and codes continuously
introducing and revising parts targeting to incorporate probabilistic methods in geotechical
practice, as an effective means to account for uncertainties that unavoidably arise in every
stage of a geotechnical project. Site investigation is located on the basis of geotechnical RBD.
A sound geotechnical analysis necessitates the systematic uncertainty quantification in the
inputs of geotechnical models, which can only be achieved through the application of more
rigorous probabilistic methods for analyzing efficiently site investigation products. Determin-
istic approaches that neglect uncertainties lead to overestimation of failure probabilities in
geotechnical structures and consequently to increased design costs and not sustainable solu-
tions. Simplistic statistical methods are not sufficient to characterize uncertainties associated
with the inherent variability of the soil material and the limited amount of data collected in
typical site investigation programs.
The complexity of the soil material is reflected in the measured properties and derived
parameters from laboratory and in-situ tests. Soil data are by nature difficult to handle and
special treatment is needed to extract valuable information from incomplete soil databases.
This study investigates the potential of multivariate probabilistic methods applied on a soil
database compiled entirely from laboratory testsвҖҷ measurements, to providing meaningful
insights on the behavior and statistical dependence of soil properties in a regional geograph-
ical scale, with ultimate goal the effective support of site-specific predictions in real-life
geotechnical applications.
Topics studied in this thesis include the compilation of a multivariate regional database, the
Bayesian framework to compute distributions of soil parameters based on measurements, the
construction of flexible multivariate distributions of correlated test indices, and the application
of the Bayesian framework along with advanced sampling algorithms to simultaneously
update several soil parameters based on site-investigation data. The thesis is arranged into 5
parts:
Chapter 2 starts with a brief introduction to the main principles of geotechnical RBD.
The importance of site investigation is highlighted and its main aspects are discussed in
detail. Extended literature review on design standards, and expertsвҖҷ opinions on uncertainty
quantification associated with site investigations, and geotechnical engineering in general, is
cited. Subsequently, an introduction to the Bayesian framework and its main elements, for
learning and updating parameters of distributions is provided. The role of soil databases and
the importance of constructing multivariate distributions of soil parameters is highlighted,
and the basic theoretical principles and assumptions are discussed. The chapter ends with
an introduction to the methods proposed in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching and
1
==PAGE:7==
1 Introduction
K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), that allow effective updating of
multivariate probabilistic models, based on the Bayesian framework and advanced sampling
techniques.
Chapter 3 presents the mathematical framework of the adopted methods in this study.
Starting with the basic terminologies of Statistical inference, the chapter builds progressively
towards the construction of joint distributions with the Nataf model and the Gaussian
copula assumption. Detailed illustrative examples are provided with analytical computations
and Python coding, and the basic methodology for simulating samples from multivariate
distributions is given. Subsequently, the multivariate distribution construction based on pre-
described JohnsonвҖҷs marginals, as proposed in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014) is presented,
and an illustrative example explaining the conventional updating process of the distribution
based on a set of site investigation measurements is provided.
In the following stage,
the chapter introduces the proposed methods by (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching,
K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), that provide an effective means to handle and extract
valuable information from soil databases, and explains the theoretical basis for constructing
"Site-specific" multivariate models, able to characterize the uncertainty in cross-correlations
between soil parameters, and to allow site-specific predictions. The basic principles of the
methods, conjugate priors, advanced sampling techniques such as the Gibbs Sampler, and
predictive distributions are explained with the assistance of Python coding.
Chapter 4 provides an overview of the constructed regional database, containing laboratory
measurements on triaxial testing parameters, and undrained shear strength from unconfined
compression tests. The basic assumptions and the preparatory data processing steps are
noted down in detail. Descriptive statistics of the regional database, along with Pearson
sample correlation coefficients are provided, and potentially correlated soil parameters are
identified to be used in the analysis.
Chapter 5 presents the results of this study. A regional multivariate Nataf distribution is
constructed based on best-fit marginal distributions. Confidence intervals for bivariate corre-
lations are provided based on simulated samples from the joint distribution. Subsequently,
a 11-parameter regional Nataf distribution is constructed based on triaxial testing measure-
ments and pre-described JohnsonвҖҷs marginals. A site-specific multivariate distribution is then
constructed from sparse measurements from a target local site. The hybridization framework
is adopted to obtain a quasi-site-specific model and simulation of soil samples is performed
to characterize cross-correlations and assist predictions at the site. Bayesian Analysis with
conjugate priors and Gibbs sampling is adopted, as well, to obtain a quasi-site-specific model.
The results of the two approaches are compared and updated Confidence Intervals for lo-
calised bivariate correlations are computed. Updated marginal distributions are provided
along with point estimates of useful marginal statistics form the simulated samples. The same
modeling approach is adopted to investigate the cross-correlations between the undrained
shear strength and soil index properties.
Chapter 6 is a summary of the most relevant results and conclusions of this thesis, and
provides recommendations for improvements in future applications.

==PAGE:8==
2 Theoretical Background
2.1 A brief introduction to Reliability Based Design in geotechnical
engineering
In a design approach that follows reliability principles, uncertain components of a system,
such as loads and soil strengths and model uncertainties are modelled as random variables,
through probability distribution functions pdfs. Figure 2.1 provides a simplified illustration of
the general stochastic problem according to K. K. Phoon, 2008.
Figure 2.1: General stochastic problem, based on K. K. Phoon, 2008
The main objective of RBD codes is the determination of the failure probability, with the
term failure referring to the case where one or a set of performance requirements are not
fulfilled. Since geotechnical RBD evolves from structural reliability, it can be useful to briefly
present the main elements of the general reliability problem from a mathematical point of
view. A failure mode of an engineering system, can be represented by a limit state function
g(X), where X = [X1; X2; ...; Xn] is the vector of all uncertain parameters included in the
analysis of the problem. These variables can be divided into resistance variables which may
be represented as function of a number of uncertain geotechnical parameters, e.g. friction
angle, undrained shear strength of a clay, and variables that represent the loads. In the case
of a complex system, e.g a geotechnical structure, in which different mechanisms can lead
to failure, multiple limit state functions must be constructed to capture the system behavior.
3
==PAGE:9==
2 Theoretical Background
In the simple case of one failure mode, the failure event corresponds to a domain в„ҰF in the
outcome space of X and is defined as (Straub, 2014a),
F = {g(X) вүӨ0} = в„ҰF.
(2.1)
The probability of X taking any value that falls in the domain в„ҰF corresponds to the probability
of failure, and thus it can be derived as the integral of the joint probability density function f (x)
of X, over в„ҰF,
Pr(F) =
Z
в„ҰF
f (x) dx1 dx2 ... dxn.
(2.2)
The reliability index can be expressed as,
ОІ = вҲ’ОҰвҲ’1 [Pr(F)]
(2.3)
where вҲ’ОҰвҲ’1 the inverse standard normal cumulative distribution function (CDF). In most
real problems, the integral of equation 2.2 cannot be evaluated analytically, e.g. for non-linear
limit state functions. It may even not be possible to use numerical integration or simplified
Monte Carlo methods when a large number of uncertain variables are involved due to high
computation cost. Considering also cases where the resistance components are derived
through Numerical analysis, e.g. Finite Elements method, the need for a more effective
computation methods can be recognized. In these cases advanced sampling techniques
should be considered to address the problemвҖҷs complexity (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2012).
2.1.1 Reliability in Design codes and standards
Design codes such as the Eurocode 7 (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004; EN 1997-2:2007: 2004), or other
standards such as Geocode 21 (Honjo et al., 2009) (Japanese Geotechnical Society), Annex
D of ISO2394: 2015 (ISO2394:1973/1986/1998/2015, 2015; K. K. Phoon and Retief, 2016),
provide the basis for implementation of probabilistic methods in geotechnical engineering. In
the European standard for geotechnical design (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004), limit state concepts and
reliability based approaches for geotechnical design (RBD) are introduced. The two design
situations, i) Ultimate Limit State, ii) Serviceability Limit State that are proposed, are based on
characteristic values and/or partial factors for the loads, capacities and soil properties. In
(EN 1997-1:2004: 2004) it is stated that "Soil variability is described via probability distribution
within the framework of uncertainty quantification instead of constant safety factors".
Calibration of partial safety factors
The approach of using partial factors in geotechnical engineering, considering ultimate
limit state, suggests that the design value is determined by dividing the characteristic value
of a geotechnicial property (e.g undrained shear strength) with a partial factor, and the
geotechnical capacity based on this design value should be larger than the design load.
According to the Eurocodes, the calibration of these partial factors can be performed using
deterministic, semi-probabilistic or full probabilistic approaches, as can be shown in Figure
4
==PAGE:10==
2 Theoretical Background
2.2. In the traditional deterministic approach, empirical rules and methods can be used to
calibrate the partial safety factors.
The probabilistic approach provides the basis for accounting for uncertainties in the model
inputs. It can be divided into two subcategories, the semi-probabilistic approach and the full
probabilistic approach, which can be adopted depending on the problem complexity, time
limitation and available resources. In the first approach, reliability methods, such as design
point methods (see First Reliability Method (FORM), SORM) (e.g. K. K. Phoon and Retief,
2016; Straub, 2014a; Honjo et al., 2009) or the Quantile Value Method (QVM) (K. K. Phoon
and Ching, 2016) can be used to calibrate resistance or partial factors and achieve a required
reliability index. In the full-probabilistic approach, reliability is derived by the probabilistic
calculation of the failure probability, the random variables are represented by probability
distributions, and more sophisticated analysis methods are utilized, i.e. simulation techniques,
e.g. Monte Carlo and subset simulation methods, and optimization with relevant to the
problem constraints. Honjo et al., 2009 summarizes the accuracy levels that can be achieved
with reliability methods, by classifying RBD into three classes as shown in table 2.1.
Level
Basic variables
Reliability evaluation
verification
III
Random variables
& probability distribution
Failure probability
Acceptable reliability level,
optimisation Economical,
environmental, etc
II
Random variables
& moments (i.e. mean,
standard deviation)
Reliability index
Target reliability index
I
Deterministic variables
Partial safety factors
Verification formula
Table 2.1: Reliability Levels according to Honjo et al., 2009

2 Theoretical Background
Characteristic values of geotechnical properties
According to Orr, T. L. and Farrell, E. R., 1999, there are three main stages in the
determination of a characteristic value for a geotechnical parameter: 1) measured value, 2)
derived value and 3) characteristic value, which are illustrated in Figure 2.3a.
(a) EC7 according to Orr, T. L. and Farrell, E. R., 1999,
based on Honjo et al., 2009
(b) Geocode 21, adapted from Honjo et al., 2009
Figure 2.3: Characteristic and design values in design standards
вҖў measured value: measured properties from site investigation in field and laboratory tests
вҖў derived value: parameters derived from transformation model (geotechnical correlation)
applied on the measured values
вҖў characteristic value: In (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004) it is stated that "characteristic values of soil
and rock properties shall take account of the variabilities of the property values". Cautious
estimate: This is a part of the guidelines in (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004) that gives rise to
many questions on how to select such values
вҖў design value: Parameters to be used in design, derived from division of characteristic
values with an associated safety factor
Despite the fact that the Eurocode guidelines point to the direction of considering statistical
methods in geotechnical applications, it is acknowledged that there is lack of detailed guidance
on how to achieve that with actual data. This is one reason that Eurocode 7 is currently
6
==PAGE:12==
2 Theoretical Background
undergoing revision, aiming to include a more detailed documentation for the facilitation of
reliability and statistical based methods in practice. The reader is referred to Orr, 2017 for
extended discussion on the selection of characteristic values and the current advancements in
the Eurocodes.
This tendency towards probabilistic analysis and adoption of reliability principles is in-
creasingly promoted by the regulators. A detailed characterization of the geological site
supported by a more in-depth probabilistic analysis can provide the basis for optimizing
design decisions in major projects and complex ground conditions. (A. Huang and Wand,
2003) highlights the significant contribution of the construction sector to the CO2 production,
as it accounts for 23% of the total emissions, sourcing from economic activities in a global
scale. More sustainable solutions, in terms of balancing safety, economic, environmental risks
and design costs, can be achieved by quantifying and maximizing the value of the information
associated with site investigation.
2.2 Uncertainties in geotechnical engineering
2.2.1 Geotechnical Site Investigation
Three types of models are typically considered in a geotechnical analysis.
The use of
engineering models is important for identifying, understanding and communicating the
critical parameters, factors and interactions typically involved in a geotechnical project.
Several representations of these models can be found in the literature (Burland, 1987;
Keaton, 2013; Sullivan, 2010; Knill, 2003; H. C. Juang, 2019) and one of these is illustrated
in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Geologic model, ground model and geotechnical model, adapted from (Keaton,
2013; H. C. Juang, 2019)
In Figure 2.4 the geologic model represents the involved site-specific geologic conditions. The
7
==PAGE:13==
2 Theoretical Background
ground model contains the geological information and the embedded engineering parameters,
which are collected during a site investigation. The geotechnical model is based on the geological
and the ground model with the derived design parameters, and it is used to estimate the
performance of the project related geotechnical structure. In (Parry et al., 2014), (IAEG)
these three models constitute the engineering geological model used for the scope of the site
geotechnical project. Keaton, 2013 emphasizes on the role of the engineering geology and
consequently the geologic model in a geotechnical site characterization study.
Preliminary geologic studies and reports on the local or regional ground conditions provide
the initial hypothesis of the site geology, which is expressed by the geologic model. These
activities can be described by the term exploration (Baecher and Christian, 2003). The
hypothesis can be revised as additional information is collected, and the initially favorable
locations for soil boring and sampling for laboratory tests can be decided through this
process. Thereafter, the additional information obtained during site-investigation can be
utilized to test and refine the engineering geologic model. In (Parry et al., 2014; Sullivan,
2010; Baynes, 1999; Stapledon, 1982; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2012) the importance of
obtaining additional information and continuously testing the model throughout all stages of
the project, including the construction and post-construction phases, known as monitoring, is
emphasized. According to Baecher and Christian, 2003, the site characterization process
contains three main stages:
вҖў reconnaissance: desk study for collecting information about the site geology
вҖў preliminarily investigation: collection of quantitative information mainly through con-
ducting a limited number of borings and geophysical surveys. The goal is to test the
qualitative hypotheses in the first stage
вҖў detailed investigation: more accurate information is obtained by intensive boring pro-
grams, detailed maps and in-situ and laboratory tests. This information is then used to
test the quantitative hypotheses of the preliminary investigation stage
Examples of sources of prior information that can be utilized during the desk-study and
site reconnaissance based on (Trautmann and F. Kulhawy, 1983; Clayton et al., 1983), are
summarized in Table 2.2.
2.2.2 Regulatory
The geological complexity of the site and the objectives of the project are the critical factors
that should be considered for the choice of the detail level in the model. The decision on the
model complexity should be in line with the general framework of promoting reliability and
ensuring an acceptable safety level. The current guidelines (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004) provide
recommendations for three main geotechnical categories:
вҖў Small and relatively simple structures (Geotechnical Category 1): Risks are considered
negligible, engineering experience and qualitative site characterization can be utilized
8

adapted from (Cao, Y. Wang, and D. Li, 2016)
вҖў Conventional structures and foundations, e.g. Embankments and earthworks (Geotech-
nical Category 2): quantitative site investigation through routine borings, field and
laboratory tests and analysis should be carried out for testing the design requirements
вҖў Major civil engineering projects in complex ground, e.g. deep excavations, tunnels in
soft ground (Geotechnical Category 3): Larger amount of information should be derived
through extensive site investigation and high level of detail should be incorporated into
the models, more sophisticated analyses
2.2.3 Sources of geotechnical uncertainty
It is acknowledged that every stage in a site characterization process, and every model in
Figure 2.4 is associated with a degree of uncertainty. The latter can be classified into two
general groups (Straub, 2020; Baecher and Christian, 2003):
вҖў Aleatory uncertainty: Uncertainties of this type cannot be reduced even as knowledge
in site investigation process. Characteristic example of uncertainty of this type in
geotechnical engineering, is the inherent variability of the soil material.
вҖў Epistemic uncertainty: The uncertainties that belong to this class can be reduced as the
state of knowledge improves. Examples of uncertainty categories that belong to this
class are the measurement error, transformation uncertainty, statistical uncertainty, knowledge
uncertainty.
Figure 2.5 was compiled from several uncertainty representations in the literature (K. K.
Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999; K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015; Baecher and Christian,
2003; Honjo et al., 2009; Cao, Y. Wang, and S. K. Au, 2011), to illustrate the main sources
ofgeotechnical uncertainty and its various subcategories.
9
==PAGE:15==
2 Theoretical Background
Figure 2.5: Propagation of geotechnical uncertainty, modified from (K. K. Phoon and F. H.
Kulhawy, 1999; K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015; Baecher and Christian, 2003;
Honjo et al., 2009; Y. Wang, Cao, and D. Li, 2016; Huber, M., 2013)
Uncertainties in the geologic model are typically associated with the limited site specific in-
formation and the knowledge, experience and ability of the responsible engineering geologist
to sufficiently identify a set of concepts that could possibly affect the specific project (Muller
and Fecker, 1979; Baynes, 1999), by utilizing regional or local maps and sections, reports and
data (Keaton, 2013; Grigoriu et al., 1987; Spry et al., 1988). Such influence factors could
be for example, the ground composition, the materials and the main geological units, the
geological history and the geological and groundwater conditions at the site. (Parry et al.,
2014; Bock, 2006; Hoek, 1999; Einstein and Baecher, 1982).
The ground model, or observational engineering geological model is dependent on the information
produced during the site-specific investigation and the quality of the proposed geologic model.
This information is constrained by the the amount and quality of the available observations
and measurements obtained from the site investigation, which are furthermore affected by
the 3D spatial and temporal fluctuations of the ground materials. Hence, this model can be
significantly affected by measurement errors and inherent soil variability, as can be seen in
Figure 2.5.
The geotechnical model most often results as a simplification of the ground model, which
involves engineering judgement and assumptions about the geological conditions, soil layers,
10
==PAGE:16==
2 Theoretical Background
soil classification and geotechnical parameters in absence of sufficient and good quality
data, resulting to additional uncertainty (transformation and statistical uncertainty) in the
engineering geological model (Keaton, 2013; Parry et al., 2014; Jaksa et al., 2003).
The standard indicator of uncertainty of a geotechnical property X can be represented by
the coefficient of variation. (K. K. Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999) pointed out that the COV
in a design parameter must be a function of the natural variability of the site, measurement
error related to the performed test type, transformation uncertainty associated with the
geotechnical correlation used to relate the site observations to the design parameter. The
statistical uncertainty that arises due to possible limited amount of site data can be considered
as an additional component of the total COV, as (LГӨnsivaara et al., 2021),
COV2
X = COV2
inh,X + COV2
err,X + COV2
trans,X + COV2
stat,X.
(2.4)
Different representations of the total COV can be found in the literature (e.g. Orr, 2017). In
(EN 1997-1:2004: 2004) the first three components of 2.4 are considered explicitly.
2.2.4 Value of Information in Site investigation
Geotechnical engineering relies heavily on "site-specificity." Sound geotechnical practice
necessitates the analysis and evaluation of site data. Design codes around the world are
continuously introducing parts that are targeting on the effective exploitation of the site
investigation products. Probabilistic Decision analysis based on site data (measurements and
observations), for quantifying the value of information that is associated with a site investigation
strategy, can be utilized to optimize the type, number, and location of observations, support
with quantitative measures the decision making (Straub, 2014b; Straub and Welpe, 2014;
Straub, 2020; Baecher and Christian, 2003).
In (ISO2394:1973/1986/1998/2015, 2015; K. K. Phoon and Retief, 2016) site investigation
is recognized as the basis for applying reliability methods in geotechnical engineering. A
through site-investigation program can provide good quality information in geotechnical
analysis and it can lead to significant reduction in the uncertainties of soil parameters, e.g. in
terms of COV reduction. K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015; Ching, D. Li, et al., 2016 suggest
that site investigation should be addressed merely as an investement item, rather than cost
element in a geotechnical projects. According to Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2012 design savings
are linked to the site investigation efforts and these are related to two typical approaches for
introducing additional information:
вҖў Quantitative increase of information by conducting several independent tests (e.g. field
CPT, SPT and laboratory tests). This approach allows cross-validation of the estimated
parameter (Cao, Y. Wang, and D. Q. Li, 2016). Uncertainties can also be reduced by
linking field and laboratory test data using existing geotechnical correlations in the
literature, e.g. (F. Kulhawy and P. Mayne, 1990; K. K. Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999;
P. W. Mayne et al., 2001; Djoenaidi, 1985). This aspect is particularly important in
case of strong dependencies between parameters, where a decision on measuring an
additional parameter can be supported by the quantification of the investment value, in
terms of COV reduction (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014).
11
==PAGE:17==
2 Theoretical Background
вҖў Qualitative increase of the information, by increasing the number of sampling/testing
locations in a specific test type. This approach can enhance precision in the estimation
of geotechnical parameters.
In addition, a good strategy should also reflect a balance between performing different
tests in different locations and conducting different tests in the same location (Ching, D.
Li, et al., 2016; K. K. Phoon, 2020). While the first approach gathers more data on the
inherent variability of the siteвҖҷs ground, the second approach introduces information about
the cross-correlation between the various test indices.
2.3 Probabilistic site characterization
Since these practices are simultaneously performed in a typical site investigation, the resulting
data usually include observations/measurements for multiple parameters. For instance,
data from boreholes often include SPT-N values and simple laboratory tests can provide
measurements for Atterberg limits. On the other hand, it is also common that a usual site
investigation program in small and medium sized projects does not provide a large number of
measurements. In addition, the collected samples correspond only to a very small proportion
of the total soil volume at the site. Limited number of laboratory tests can be performed
on the soil samples as well, due to the available budget constraints (Ching, K. K. Phoon,
Khan, et al., 2020; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Baecher and Christian, 2003; Nawari
and Liang, 2000; Y. Wang, S. Au, et al., 2010).
One can argue that it is not possible to obtain meaningful statistics, such as ranges for
the mean values with confidence, which for example can be used for the determination of
characteristic values according to (EN 1997-1:2004: 2004). An experienced engineer may be
able to combine information from literature and data from other sources, which can judge as
similar to the site at hand, to extend the limited site-specific information (K. K. Phoon and
Retief, 2016). As pointed out, the soil data are in general not independent (K. K. Phoon and
Retief, 2016; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2012), and thus in geotechnical practice, correlations
(transformation models) for the derivation of a design parameter are adopted, such as for
instance, derivation of friction angle of sand from SPT-N values. However, in the absence of
sufficient and good quality site investigation data, it is very difficult to create local correlations
with confidence. In this context global correlations from literature are usually utilized.
The connection between the various uncertainties sources in Figure 2.5 and these usual
practices is evident:
1. Every performed test is associated with errors, which are reflected in the measured
properties.
2. Significant statistical uncertainty arises if one relies on limited measurements to obtain
reliable statistics
3. It is not a trivial task to identify and incorporate in the study information from com-
parable sites. Advanced engineering geology background may be needed to identify
comparable ground conditions in different published studies and data.
12
==PAGE:18==
2 Theoretical Background
4. One has to be very cautious when applying generic correlations (e.g. (F. Kulhawy and
P. Mayne, 1990; K. K. Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999), since they are usually based
on databases compiled from many sites with various soil types and conditions and
they can introduce a large amount of transformation uncertainty (Ching, K. K. Phoon,
Khan, et al., 2020; EN 1997-1:2004: 2004).
5. Construction of local correlations based on limited site specific data can introduce a
significant amount of statistical uncertainty.
6. Soils are naturally variable not only between different locations at a site but even in
the same homogenous layers, as well (Baecher and Christian, 2003). The spatial
variability cannot be quantified with simplified methods
2.3.1 Bayesian Framework
The systematic quantification of uncertainties in every stage of a geotechnical project necessi-
tates the adoption of more rigorous probabilistic analyses. Bayesian Analysis is increasingly
gaining attention among researchers and practitioners, as it provides the framework to effec-
tively incorporate and update prior engineering knowledge and information about ground
parameters, using site investigation products (measurements, data) to draw inferences from
them and explicitly quantify and possibly reduce the involved uncertainties. Apart from
parameters updating from sampling/measurements, known as Bayesian Inference, many
studies have shown that Bayesian methods can be applied to effectively update geotechnical
models, (Bayesian model updating) (Straub, 2020), while they are powerful tools in assessing
the performance of geotechnical systems (e.g. (Papaioannou and Straub, 2012; L. Zhang,
2004; J. Zhang et al., 2012; L. Wang et al., 2012; J. Zhang et al., 2009; Cheung and W. H. Tang,
2005; L. Zhang, 2004)) and well suited for Inverse Analysis to utilize observations collected
through monitoring (Straub, 2020).
Two approaches can be adopted in the Bayesian framework for modeling spatially variable
parameters. In the first method the parameter is modeled by a random variable through a
probability distribution function (PDF). The PDF of the soil parameter reflects the inherent
variability at any location within an assumed statistical homogenous area, i.e within a
homogenous soil layer. Based on prior information and site data it is possible to estimate
and update the statistical moments of the distribution and possibly reduce the statistical
uncertainty, in terms of COV reduction. On the other hand, the spatial dependence between
different locations in the area of interest is not explicitly modeled and the inherent variability
cannot be reduced (e.g. (Straub and Papaioannou, 2015; Papaioannou and Straub, 2012;
Y. Wang and Cao, 2013).
Explicit characterization of the inherent variability can only be achieved with the random
field approach (Papaioannou and Straub, 2012; Straub, Papaioannou, and Betz, 2017;
Y. Wang, Cao, and D. Li, 2016). Considering the basic case of 1-D spatial fluctuation (i.e.
vertical direction), the variable parameter is modeled by a random field X(z) at each location
z, e.g. (Straub and Papaioannou, 2015; Baecher and Christian, 2008; Papaioannou and
Straub, 2017). The random field consists of discrete set of spatially correlated random
13
==PAGE:19==
2 Theoretical Background
variables at each location, which are represented by a marginal PDF and its parameters, and
a correlation function with a scale of fluctuation parameter, is used to describe the spatial
dependence structure. Figure illustrates the main elements of the Bayesian framework in the
site characterization process, as summarized in (Y. Wang, Cao, and D. Li, 2016).
Figure 2.6: Bayesian updating of a soil parameter, adapted from (Y. Wang, Cao, and D. Li,
2016)
Let fX(x|Оё) denote the PDF of an uncertain geotechnical parameter X dependent on the
unknown distribution parameters Оё. The main components of the Bayesian framework, shown
in 2.6 can be briefly summarized in the following points:
вҖў Prior distribution: The prior distribution can be denoted as (Straub, 2020),
f вҖІ(Оё) = f (Оё)
(2.5)
where Оё corresponds to the vector of the uncertain parameters of the model. Desk-study
and site reconnaissance provide the prior knowledge which is reflected in a quantitative
manner in this distribution. Hence, the prior distribution reflects the uncertainty in
the parameters Оё prior to any site investigation. When the quality and/or amount of
information about the parameter is low, weakly or non-informative priors are selected,
which reflect the large statistical uncertainty (e.g. Cao, Y. Wang, and D. Q. Li, 2016. As
information increases, more sophisticated types of distributions can be utilized.
вҖў Likelihood function, denoted by
Li(Оё) = fX(xi|Оё),
(2.6)
14
==PAGE:20==
2 Theoretical Background
where xi, i = 1, ..., n corresponds to a measurement of the continuous quantity X. The
likelihood function is essential element of the Bayesian framework. It describes the
probability of the measurement/observation outcome, or in other words, it defines
"how likely" is a measurement or observation xi conditional on the possible state of the
uncertain parameters of a probabilistic model Оё (e.g. statistical moments in PDF, scale
of fluctuation in auto-correlation function) (Straub, 2020; Papaioannou and Straub,
2017). If n independent measurements are obtained, the total likelihood is defined as the
product of individual likelihoods (Straub and Papaioannou, 2015),
L(Оё) =
n
вҲҸ

fX(xi|Оё).
(2.7)
The formulation of the likelihood function depends on i) the type of the probabilistic
modeling approach (random variable or random field), which reflects the inherent
variability of the geotechnical parameter and ii) the deterministic transformation model
used to derive the parameter, in case this was not directly measured in a field/lab test
(Cao, Y. Wang, and D. Li, 2016). The latter is subjected to transformation uncertainty.
вҖў Posterior distribution of Оё, given the measurements x = (x1, ..., xn), denoted by
f вҖІвҖІ(Оё) = f (Оё|x),
(2.8)
Using the Bayes rule, the posterior distribution is proportional to the likelihood function
and the prior distribution,
f вҖІвҖІ(Оё) = cL(Оё) f вҖІ(Оё)
(2.9)
where c is a proportionality constant which ensures that the posterior distribution in-
tegrates to 1, i.e. it is a valid PDF. The posterior distribution quantifies the influence
of the site data to the uncertain soil parameters of the model. In low dimensional
problems, the proportionality constant can be calculated with numerical integration. In
the special cases where conjugate priors can be used to describe the prior distribution of
the parameters, analytical formulation of the posterior distribution is feasible. However,
in problems that involve many parameters, sampling methods are used for calculating
the posterior PDF.
If more data are obtained in a later stage of site investigation, or by monitoring (e.g. mea-
surements of deformations) in construction and post-construction phases (Straub and Pa-
paioannou, 2015), it is possible to sequentially update the prior information. In this case
the posterior PDF derived in the previous step serves as prior PDF for the next updating
process. Several studies have investigated the benefits of Bayesian methods for constructing a
site-specific probabilistic model to estimate a single design soil/rock parameter (e.g. (Y. Wang
and Cao, 2013; Y. Wang, S. Au, et al., 2010; X. Feng and Jimenez, 2014; Cao and Y. Wang,
2014)

==PAGE:21==
2 Theoretical Background
2.3.2 Multivariate distribution of dependent random variables
When multivariate data are available in a sufficient quantity, one should consider the con-
struction of a multivariate distribution for the site (e.g. (K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2013;
Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015). A multivariate probabilistic
model offers extended capabilities to site characterization, since it can include information
from all relevant test indices produced in site investigation. Motivations for constructing a
multivariate distribution for practical applications can be for example:
вҖў The estimation of the statistical moments of a design parameter, given multivariate
information (more than one measured parameters) from site investigation
вҖў The consistent modeling of the dependency between soil parameters (e.g. cross correla-
tion), which adds more information than the univariate distribution statistics (mean,
variance, COV). New strong correlations may be discovered, while generic transforma-
tion models can be "localized" to provide more realistic information about the site at
hand. Calculation of updated Confidence Intervals (CI) for the localized "bivariate"
models can reduce the transformation uncertainty in design parameters (e.g. (Ching
and K. K. Phoon, 2014)).
Hence, a multivariate probabilistic model can allow the approximation of the true behavior of
the ground parameters at the site, as closely as possible. However, this comes not without
cost, since constructing a multivariate model is more complex task than defining marginal
distributions.
The most common method to construct a multivariate distribution makes use of the
Gaussian copula concept (e.g. (Embrechts et al., 2001; Nelsen, 2006; Straub, 2014a)). When
the dependent random variables can be described by a Gaussian copula, the Nataf model
(Nataf, 1962; P. L. Liu and Der Kiureghian, 1986) or "translation method" (D. Q. Li, S. B. Wu,
et al., 2012) can be used to construct the multivariate distribution. The initial step in this
method is the marginal transformation of all uncertain parameters to from their physical space
to standard normal space. Let Yi denote an uncertain, non-Normal soil parameter. This can
be translated to an equivalent standard normal variable Xi with the following transformation:
T : Yi вҶ’Xi = ОҰвҲ’1 [FYi(yi)] ,
(2.10)
where Fi denotes the known cumulative distribution function (CDF) of Yi and ОҰвҲ’1 corresponds
to the inverse standard Normal CDF. The set of the transformed variables are correlated
and they assumed to be jointly Normal (Gaussian) distributed. One reason for making this
assumption is that it allows to take advantage of the computationally beneficial properties of
the multivariate Normal distribution. In reality it should be not expected that all geotech-
nical properties are indeed jointly normal distributed, even if they have been marginally
transformed to the normal space. However, by assuming joint normality, the underlying
multivariate normal distribution can be entirely defined for given marginal distributions, i.e.
distribution function of single parameters, and a symmetric correlation coefficient matrix,
let it denoted by RYY, which is usually calculated directly from the data. The most popular
16
==PAGE:22==
2 Theoretical Background
statistic for dependence is the Pearson correlation coefficient which provides a measure of
linear dependency between the uncertain parameters.
The pairwise correlation coefficients in the correlation matrix CXX of the transformed
variables Xi are not identical with the corresponding ones of the Yi (Straub, 2014a) variables
in original space. The Nataf model involves the decomposition of the correlation matrix RXX,
known as the Cholesky decomposition. This is not trivial and the method can collide for
strongly correlated parameters or in high dimensional problems, due to positive-definite
matrix (positive eigenvalues) restriction (e.g. (Lebrun and Dutfoy, 2009b; Trefethen and
Bau, 1997; Lyche, 2020; Straub, 2014a)).
Other transformation methods do exist, such as the Rosenblatt (Rosenblatt, 1952; Hohen-
bichler and Rackwitz, 1981; Lebrun and Dutfoy, 2009a; F. Wang et al., 2017; X. Z. Wu,
2013a) and the Hermite polynomials transformation (Lemaire, 2009; K. K. Phoon, 2006),
which can be applicable in more complex dependency types of variables. Applications of the
latter transformation method can be found in (e.g. (Sudret, 2015; Sudret, 2008; Tsilifis et al.,
2019)).
Apart from the Gaussian copulas, other types such as t, Frank, Clayton, Gumbel, and
Plackett copulas have been proposed in geotechnical engineering literature, to address cases
where the Gaussian copula type cannot adequately describe the dependence structure between
the parameters (e.g (X. Z. Wu, 2013a; X. Z. Wu, 2013b; X. S. Tang et al., 2013; Uzielli and
P. W. Mayne, 2011; D. Q. Li, L. Zhang, et al., 2015; X. S. Tang et al., 2013; D. Q. Li, X. S. Tang,
et al., 2013; D. Q. Li, S. B. Wu, et al., 2012). However, most of the studies focus on bivariate
since these copulas are mainly used for modeling bivariate dependence (pair copulas). More
recently, the promising for geotechnical applications, vine copula model has been applied to
multidimensional problems (e.g. (Jiang et al., 2015; LГј et al., 2020)). Vine copulas can be
obtained as products of conditional pair copulas (Aas et al., 2009).
An advantage of copulas is that they can provide dependent simulated samples from the
joint distribution of the parameters of interest. The selection of marginal distributions for
the input geotechnical parameters is important for constructing the multivariate distribution
with copulas. They can accommodate not only the well known in geotechnical practice,
normal and lognormal distributions, but the uncertain parameters can follow different types
of distributions. An identification of the best-fit marginal distribution can be carried out
before applying the copula theory. A best-fit copula step can be then performed to select the
copula type which better describes the multivariate dependence structure (e.g. (D.-Q. Li and
X.-S. Tang, 2015)).
2.3.3 The Multivariate distribution conditioning
Bayesian updating methods can be even more effective and powerful when implemented
within the context of a multivariate model. Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014 describe three
levels of updating that can be achieved in site characterization:
вҖў Level I: Common practice of estimating the mean value of a design parameter
17
==PAGE:23==
2 Theoretical Background
вҖў Level II: Includes the uncertainty characterization of a single uncertain parameter with
estimation of the marginal distribution and its parameters (see section 2.3.1)
вҖў Level III: Construction of the conditional multivariate distribution of the measured and
design parameters.
Even when the measured data are weakly dependent, the uncertainties in design parameters
can be reduced when they are conditioned on a larger number of test indices (K. K. Phoon
and Retief, 2016). The combination and coupling of multiple parameters in a systematic
way, is based on a multivariate distribution model constructed from a soil database. This
model can serve as "prior" distribution, and can be updated by utilizing site investigation
data. The result would be a site-specific multivariate posterior distribution which can then be
used for more realistic design approaches, resulting to more design savings and supporting
geotechnical RBD (e.g (K. K. Phoon and Retief, 2016)).
K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2013 proposed the construction of a multivariate model based on
the Nataf model, by making use of the JohnsonвҖҷs family of distributions as marginals for the
input parameters. The advantage of using these distributions is that they provide a closed
form expression of the transformation of the random variables into the standard normal
space, Equation 2.10 (and the inverse to original space), and they maintain their distribution
type when they are conditioned to some site investigation data (conditional distribution).
This is important since it ensures that the constructed Multi-normal distribution, will be again
Multi-normal when conditioned on some site investigation data. The conditional distribution
can provide more precise confidence intervals. Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014 proposed a
sampling method, called bootstrap, for generating correlation coefficient pairs that can address
the positive-definite correlation matrix restriction in the Nataf transformation, while also
accounting for the statistical uncertainty in the correlation coefficients.
2.3.4 Bayesian Machine Learning: The baseline model
As pointed out in section 2.3, site investigation data present some relative unique features
to geotechnical site characterization. K. K. Phoon, 2018 describes the site investigation data
attributes using the acronym MUSIC:
вҖў Multivariate (M): Measurements and observations for multiple parameters are typically
collected in site investigation. A multivariate data-set can be viewed for example as a
table in an Excel spreadsheet containing N columns that correspond to the measured
soil parameters at M locations (e.g. several depths).
вҖў Unique (U): This aspect is related to the relative uniqueness of each site characteristics,
which is reflected in the collected data.
вҖў Sparse (S): In the sense that field and laboratory tests are conducted at limited depths
and few locations, and hence, limited amount of data are usually obtained
18
==PAGE:24==
2 Theoretical Background
вҖў Incomplete (I): This characteristic refers to the absence of simultaneous information on
every soil property in a data-set. This could be imagined as a matrix MxN with missing
(or NaN) entries in several positions.
вҖў possibly Corrupted (C): This attribute refers to possible bad measurements and outliers
in the data-set.
These data attributes pose significant difficulties to every data-driven site characterization
methods. (K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015) describes this as the "ugly data challenge".
K. K. Phoon and Retief, 2016; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014 suggest that a sound practice
in site-characterization should attempt to exploit in an effective way all available multivariate
information, including both site investigation data and published data in literature. Such
an approach could be effective to dealing with MUSIC site investigation data, by utilizing
typically abundant published data that are not directly related to the site at hand (K. K.
Phoon, 2018). (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2020a) refer to these data as "Big Indirect Data" to
underscore that the data sparsity problem is associated only with a local site. Types of BID
can be for instance (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020):
вҖў A municipal database containing measurements and observations on soil properties
from sites located in the same city
вҖў A regional database containing data from sites that belong to the same geographical
area. This region can extend from different towns to a few neighboring countries (e.g.
Germany, Austria, Switzerland)
вҖў A global database that contains data from a large number of sites from many geograph-
ical regions.
Utilization of BID necessitates the adoption of more demanding data analysis methods for
handling large databases. Engineering judgement cannot be effective in extracting all relevant
to the site information, that may be hidden in large multivariate data-sets. Simplified data-
driven methods cannot be effective in exploiting and quantifying the value of information in
BID. (K. Phoon et al., 2021) refers to this aspect as the "Site Recognition challenge".
A Bayesian Machine Learning method, i.e based solely on data, was proposed recently
(Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) for the construction of a multivariate PDF based on a clay
soil database, containing a large number of sites with measurements on CPT parameters and
index properties. The method evolves from previous studies of the authors (K. K. Phoon
and Ching, 2013; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014) and introduces a baseline probabilistic
site-specific model that can be effective under MUSIC data constraints. The method is based
again on the multi-Normal assumption. (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching, K. K. Phoon,
Khan, et al., 2020) proposed a Bayesian updating framework under which a quasi-site-specific
model can be constructed with a hybridization of a generic multivariate distribution (from
BID) and a site-specific model constructed from the site data.
In this approach different sets of parameters, (Вөdb, Cdb) and (Вөs, Cs) (i.e. means and correla-
tion matrices), are considered for the generic database and the site-specific model, respectively.
19
==PAGE:25==
2 Theoretical Background
These are considered unknown and they are inferred from the generic database and site data,
respectively. The statistical uncertainty in the site-specific parameters (Вөs, Cs) can be very
high due to limited amount of available site investigation data. Hence, Bayesian analysis is
needed to explicitly quantify the statistical uncertainty. With regard to the generic parameters
(Вөdb, Cdb) , if the amount of data is very large, the statistical uncertainty in the parameters
can be low, meaning that Bayesian analysis in this case is not necessary (case in (Ching
and K. K. Phoon, 2019), where the global database used contains a very large number of
data points). However, if the compiled database does not contain a large enough number of
data points, quantification of the statistical uncertainty should be carried out for the generic
distribution parameters, as well.
The main purpose of applying Bayesian analysis is the derivation of the posterior distribution
of the uncertain parameters of the probability distribution function (see 2.3.1). In multivariate
analysis updating all parameters and derive their posterior distribution can be very computa-
tional challenging, and conventional numerical integration in high dimensions (e.g. larger
than 3 parameters) is not effective. It is possible though, to reduce significantly the compu-
tational effort by adopting some special types of prior distributions, called conjugate priors.
When conjugate priors describe each uncertain parameter of the prior distribution, analytical
formulation of their posterior distributions is feasible (Givens and Hoeting, 2013; Gelman
et al., 2013; Ang and W. Tang, 2007; Straub and Papaioannou, 2015; ISSMGE-TC205/304,
2017).
In problems that involve a large number of parameters, sampling methods can be utilized
for efficient computation. Such simulation techniques are the Markov chain Monte Carlo methods,
or MCMC (Hastings, 1970; Metropolis et al., 1953; Gilks et al., 1996). Exact sampling
from the posterior distribution of the parameters is possible when adopting conjugate priors,
which exist under the multi-Normal distribution assumption. The authors in (Ching and
K. K. Phoon, 2019) adopted the Gibbs Sampler algorithm that belongs to the MCMC methods,
in conjunction with conjugate priors, to sequentially generate samples from the conditional
(posterior) distribution of the parameters (Вөdb, Cdb) and (Вөs, Cs). The Gibbs Sampler is also a
particularly useful technique that can handle the incomplete feature of the data (Ching, Muto,
et al., 2006; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019).
The key parts of the proposed hybridization framework can be summarized in the following
steps (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020):
вҖў Construction of the underlying generic multivariate probability density function condi-
tioned on the complete set of data points in the municipal/regional/global database.
Let f (x|Xdb,o) symbolize the conditional generic distribution with parameters (Вөdb, Cdb).
The unknown parameters are divided into groups and conditional samples can be
generated from their posterior distribution, i.e. conditioned on the observed data, using
the Gibbs Sampler (Gilks et al., 1996; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019). The database
PDF can be expressed as a mixture of multivariate normal PDFs with parameters the
(Вөdb, Cdb) samples (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020).
вҖў Construction of the underlying site-specific multivariate probability density function
conditioned on the complete set of data points in the site data. Let f (x|Xs,o) symbolize
20
==PAGE:26==
2 Theoretical Background
the conditional generic distribution with parameters (Вөs, Cs). The unknown parameters
are divided into groups and conditional samples can be generated from their posterior
distribution, i.e. conditioned on the observed data, using the Gibbs Sampler (Gilks et al.,
1996; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014). The database PDF is a mixture of multivariate
normal PDFs with parameters the (Вөs, Cs) samples (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al.,
2020).
вҖў Hybridization between the database PDF and the site specific PDF,
f (x|hb) вҲқf (x|Xdb,o) Г— f (x|Xs,o)
(2.11)
The resulting hybrid PDF can be used for enhanced site-specific predictions (Ching,
K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020).
Studies based on such soil/rock multivariate databases can be found in the literature (Ching
and K. Phoon, 2012; Ching, K. Phoon, and C. Chen, 2014; DвҖҷIgnazzio et al., 2016; S. Liu
et al., 2016; J. R. Chen, 2004; Ching, LI, et al., 2018; S. Feng and Vardanega, 2019b; S. Feng
and Vardanega, 2019a; LГ¶fman and Korkiala-Tanttu, 2021; Ching, K. K. Phoon, HO,
et al., 2020; D. Zhang et al., 2020; Xiao et al., 2021; Ching, Lin, et al., 2017; Ching and
K. K. Phoon, 2014). These are open-access databases and are provided by the committee of
"Engineering Practice of Risk Assessment and Management (TC304)" - "International Society
for Soil Mechanics and Geotechnical Engineering (ISSMGE)" (www-01 ).
2.3.5 Recent advancements in Data-driven Site characterization
(Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2020b) extended the model and proposed a "similarity index"
method that is partially able to provide a numerical measure to the site-uniqueness data
feature, for exploiting a global database. These set of methods are based on the assumption
that the measurements between different depths are independent and they do not explicitly
characterize the spatial variability. (Zheng et al., 2021) proposed a probabilistic outlier
detection method, to reduce the influence of bad quality data in the characterization of the
statistical uncertainty of the parameters.
In (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2020a) the BML model (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) was
improved to account for the spatial variability of the site properties among depths (MUSIC-X).
(Ching, W. H. Huang, et al., 2021) developed a Sparse Bayesian Learning method that can
work under realistic and complex 3D setting, but it considers only univariate data (CPT data).
The most recent and advanced data-driven site characterizations methods that can work under
realistic and complex 3D setting (MUSIC-3X), while also attempting to effectively address
the complex "site recognition" challenge were proposed very recently (Ching, W. H. Huang,
et al., 2021; Ching, WU, et al., 2021; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Yang, et al., 2021).
The various aspects of dependency between multivariate data cannot be ignored in realistic
scenarios. An ideal site characterization approach, should target to explicitly characterize the
following dependency structures between the multivariate site data (K. Phoon et al., 2021):
21
==PAGE:27==
2 Theoretical Background
1. cross-correlation between the different parameters (e.g index properties and cone
resistance data at the same location) (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching, Lin, et al.,
2017; K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019)
2. spatial correlation, e.g. undrained shear strength data at different locations (Ching and
K. K. Phoon, 2020a)
3. spatial-cross correlations, e.g index properties and cone resistance data at different
locations (Ching, W. H. Huang, et al., 2021)

==PAGE:28==
3 Methods Description
3.1 Learning parameters of probability distributions from data
This section focuses on the main elements of Statistical inference and the respective techniques
which can be used to learn parameters of probability distributions. Three methods are
typically used to estimate the parameters of a probabilistic model:
1. Method of moments (MoM)
2. Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE)
3. Bayesian parameters estimation
3.1.1 Method of Moments (MoM)
The method of moments refers to the most common practice of estimating the parameters of
a distribution from the sample moments. Let X denote the uncertain soil parameter modelled
as random variable and xi, i = 1, ..., n the measurements (samples) obtained from a field/lab
test. For probability distributions that require two parameters to be defined, it is sufficient to
calculate the first two moments, i.e. sample mean x and sample variance s2,
x = 1
n

вҲ‘
n=1

(3.1)
s2 =
1
n вҲ’1

(xi вҲ’x)2.
(3.2)
Let the parameters of the distribution of X be denoted by Оё. With the MoM they can be
derived by utilizing x and s2 as point estimators of the mean (expected value) E[X] = ВөX
and variance Var[X] = Пғ2
X of the random variable X. higher moments such as skewness
(normalized 3rd moment), kurtosis (4th), can be utilized as well.
In certain cases, it can be also possible to calculate the statistical uncertainty in the point
estimates ЛҶОё of the parameters Оё of a distribution, obtained with the MoM. This corresponds
to treating the sample mean and sample variance as random variables and calculating their
expected value and variance. However, there are important limitations in quantifying the
statistical uncertainty with the MoM and this method is not appropriate when limited amount
of data are available. The true mean and variance of an uncertain soil parameter X can
be significantly different from the values obtained from Equation 3.1 and Equation 3.2,
respectively, when the amount of data is low. For more details on the MoM estimator the
reader is referred to (Kottegoda and Rosso, 2008; Straub, 2020).

==PAGE:29==
3 Methods Description
3.1.2 Maximum Likelihood Estimation
The MLE method is a more preferable approach than the MoM for learning parameters of a
probability distribution. It can accommodate different types of data, it can handle correlated
observations and it provides a better means for quantifying the statistical uncertainty in the
estimator (Aldrich, 1997; Kottegoda and Rosso, 2008). The MLE approach is also applicable
to more general types of models, and it can be used to learn parameters of a geotechnical
correlation (or regression models in general). In learning the parameters of a transformation
model, MLE can provide estimates of the transformation error uncertainty (e.g. Straub, 2020).
Another practical application of MLE is the estimation of model parameters from censored
data while the method can also provide a quantitative measure to decide the probabilistic
model that better describes an uncertain soil parameter, among a set of competing models
(e.g. C. H. Juang et al., 2015; Kottegoda and Rosso, 2008).
Consider a continuous random variable X representing an uncertain soil parameter with
PDF fX(x). A set of measurements x1, x2, ..., xn is obtained from a field/lab test. Each
measurement xi corresponds to a realization of the PDF of X. Consider also Оё = Оё corresponds
to a vector of unknown, but nonrandom distribution parameters. The PDF of X can be written
as fX(x|Оё) to explicitly indicate the dependency on its parameters Оё. The chance to observe a
single measurement xi, given a series of possible values for the parameters Оё is described by
the likelihood function 2.6, which is a function of Оё (since the measurement has already been
made). It is re-written here for consistency reasons,
Li(Оё) = fX(xi|Оё),
For a data-set containing n (assumed) statistically independent measurements (x1, x2, ..., xn),
the joint likelihood is defined as the product of individual likelihood functions (Equation 2.7),
L(Оё) =

The principle of MLE lies in deriving the parameter values ЛҶОё that maximize the plausibility of
data (x1, x2, ..., xn). The selection of the optimal set of values ЛҶОё can be achieved by maximizing
the joint likelihood function of Equation 2.7,
ЛҶОё = arg max

point estimates. Two useful statistical properties of MLE are:
24

вҖў As the number of observations increases the ML estimators ЛҶОё approach the Normal
distribution. This property follows from the Central limit theorem.
вҖў The ML estimators are unbiased for a large number of observations, i.e the mean values
of ЛҶОё approach the true values.
As pointed out in section 2.3, statistical uncertainty arises due to limited amount of data typi-
cally available on a soil parameter. Hence, though MLE cannot quantify the true uncertainty,
but it can provide an approximation based on the covariance matrix of the ML estimators ЛҶОё.

provide good estimates and its limitations the reader is referred to (Papoulis and Pillai,
2002; Givens and Hoeting, 2005; Aldrich, 1997; Straub, 2020; Kottegoda and Rosso, 2008)
3.1.3 Bayesian estimation
A brief discussion on the Bayesian framework was made in section 2.3.1. The bayesian
parameter estimation facilitates the explicit characterization of the statistical uncertainty in
the parameters of a distribution and it is a suggested method when limited amount of data on
an uncertain soil parameter is available. In MoM and MLE methods the unknown parameters
Оё are treated as constant-deterministic values and point estimates are computed based solely
on the measurements xi. In contrast, the bayesian approach enables the exploitation of prior
information on the uncertain parameters of a probabilistic model in the estimation process.
This prior knowledge is reflected in the prior distribution f вҖІ(Оё), Equation 2.5, the unknown
parameters Оё are treated as random variables and a full posterior distribution f вҖІвҖІ
Оё = fОё|x of the
parameters is obtained by updating the density f вҖІ(Оё) based on the data, following Bayes rule
and Equation 2.9,
f вҖІвҖІ(Оё) = cL(Оё) f вҖІ(Оё)
where L(Оё) the likelihood function of Equation 2.6. As pointed out in section 2.3.1, the posterior
distribution reflects the inherent variability of the a measure soil parameter at a location

Desk-study and site reconnaissance provide the prior knowledge which is quantitatively
represented by the prior distribution. The choice of the prior distribution is important,
it affects the way in which the posterior is computed and has significant influence in the
prediction quality (Straub and Papaioannou, 2015; Straub, 2020). Depending on the amount
and quality of the information collected prior to any site investigation, different types of prior
distributions may be selected.
Improper and non-informative priors: Uniform distribution
The so called improper priors correspond to improper density functions meaning that they
do not integrate to 1. For example the uniform distribution from 0 to вҲһis an improper PDF

The complexity of the integral computation in Equation 3.9 depends on the type of the

unknown parameters.
However, when a large number of parameters are involved the
computational effort increases and the derivation of the constant c becomes impractical. In
these cases, sampling methods such as the MCMC methods can be used to generate samples
from a target distribution and the computation of proportionality constant is not needed (e.g.
Straub and Papaioannou, 2015).
Point estimators can also be computed from the posterior maximum, known also as MAP
estimator (Maximum A-Posteriori). The MAP estimators are calculated by maximizing the
posterior f вҖІвҖІ(Оё), i.e finding its mode,

The computation of the proportionality constant is not needed in this case, which significantly
reduces the computational effort. However when high statistical uncertainty is present the
posterior mean, Equation 3.7, provides better point estimates of the parameters (Straub, 2020).
For non-informative prior distributions, the mode of the posterior is identical with the MLE
obtained from Equation 3.3. In these cases, the likelihood dominates the posterior.
In Table 2.2 some activities that can produce an amount of useful information during the
desk study and site reconnaissance stages of site investigation were presented. This prior
knowledge can be further improved by incorporating sound engineering judgement from
professionals with experience on the geology of the region (e.g. Vick, 2002) As information
on a soil property X and its distribution parameters Оё increases, improper types of prior PDFs
can be replaced by non-informative priors. The integration of prior information is critical for
geotechnical RBD (e.g. K. K. Phoon and Retief, 2016). Even if the amount of information on
a soil parameter is small, non-informative priors can be a means to quantitatively introduce
a degree of engineering judgement, as for instance in defining the physical boundaries of a
property with respect to the soil type.
Typical selection of non-informative priors is the joint uniform distribution. The uniform
distribution assigns equal probability of occurence to the distribution parameters that take
values in a specific interval [Оұ, ОІ]. The values Оұ, ОІ can be related to the minimum and
maximum values of the parameter that had been observed in past studies at the site at hand,
or other sites in the geographical region, or they can be selected based on typical ranges
of the mean, standard deviation or COV reported in literature, related to some specific site
conditions, e.g same soil type. (Cao, Y. Wang, and D. Li, 2016) compiled information on soil
parameters from several sources in literature (e.g. (K. K. Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999;
Lacasse and Nadim, 1996; Baecher, 1983; Lumb, 1974; Rackwitz, 2000; Uzielli, Lacasse,
et al., 2007; Akbas and F. Kulhawy, 2010)). Prior information on the statistics of relevant
to the current study soil parameters is presented below in Table 3.1. The prior range for
estimates of the mean ВөвҖІ are defined from the minimum and maximum values of Вө reported
in literature. The prior estimates of ПғвҖІ are derived as a function of ВөвҖІ and the reported COV
of each property. The prior ranges in 3.1 refer to fine-grained soils. More specific soil types
with narrower bounds can be found in (K. K. Phoon and F. H. Kulhawy, 1999). Based on
such ranges of statistics, it is trivial to define a joint uniform distribution. For example, in
case of two unknown distribution parameters (Оё1, Оё2), a non-informative prior f вҖІ

3.1.4 The Predictive distribution
The posterior distribution f вҖІвҖІ(Оё|x1, ..., xn) characterizes the uncertainty in the parameters

variability of the random variable X (i.e the uncertain geotechnical property) which is modeled

posterior predictive PDF is computed following Equation

3.2 Selection of probability models
In many cases there exist more than one probabilistic model that can be used to model
an uncertain geotechnical parameter X. For example, different probability distribution are
proposed in the literature for modeling the Mohr-Coulomb soil strength parameters c and
П•, denoting the cohesion and friction angle respectively. Some of them include the normal,
lognormal, Weibull, Gamma, Gumbel, Beta probability distributions e.g. (D.-Q. Li and X.-S.
Tang, 2015; X. S. Tang et al., 2013; Baecher and Christian, 2003). Hence, the question is
how to select the best model among a set of candidate models M1, ..., Mm, based on the measurements
and observed data? Two different approaches are described here:
1. Model comparison and selection with MLE based on: i) the Akaike Information Criterion
(AIC) and ii) the Bayesian Information Criterion (BIC)
2. Bayesian model selection.
As pointed out in Section 3.1.2, the ML approach can provide a measure that facilitates the
comparison between different candidate models Mi, i = 1, ..., n. The approach of modelsвҖҷ
comparison in with MLE can be achieved through the following steps:
вҖў Derivation of the ML estimators of the parameters following Equation 3.3 for each
candidate model, denoted here by ЛҶОёMi
вҖў Evaluation of the log-likelihood function of each model from Equation 3.4 at the MLE
values ЛҶОёMi, i.e. lMi(ЛҶОёMi)
Following these two steps, the Akaike Information Criterion (AIC) can be derived from,
AIC = 2k вҲ’2lMi(ЛҶОёMi)
(3.14)
29
==PAGE:35==
3 Methods Description
and the Bayesian Information Criterion (BIC) from,
BIC = k ln n вҲ’2lMi(ЛҶОёMi),
(3.15)
where k corresponds to the number of parameters and n the number of data-points. The
model with the lowest value of AIC or BIC is the the favorable one among the candidate
models M1, ..., Mm. Both AIC and BIC are criteria used to assess the loss of information that
occurs when fitting inappropriate models to the data. For instance, MLE tends to favor more
complex models (leading to larger likelihood lMi(ЛҶОёMi), e.g. models with larger number of
parameters, instead of simpler ones, which can lead to overfitting the data. For this reason the
proposed criteria, introduce a degree of penalization to more complex models. While the first
terms in Equations 3.14, 3.15 are related to the quality of the model in terms of the number
of involved parameters, the second terms reflect the penalization factor to more complex
models. These criteria offer a computationally simple method to select appropriate models to
characterize a geotechnical parameter. However in some cases, such as when limited amount
of data are available and the statistical uncertainty is high, they may oversimplify the problem
and the model which gives the minimum BIC or AIC, may not be the most proper one among
the set of candidates (Straub, 2020).
In such cases, it is recommended to apply a Bayesian approach, known as Bayesian model
class selection, to compare different models (Straub, 2020). Apart from comparing different
univariate probability distribution types, the Bayesian model class selection is applicable
to comparing different types copulas (X. S. Tang et al., 2013) and more general types of
models, as well (Yuen, 2010). In this approach a prior probability is assigned to each of the
candidate models Mi, i = 1, ...m, which is then updated based on the observed data. The
models are assumed collectively exhaustive (see set theory) which means that it must hold
вҲ‘m
i=1 Pr(Mi) = 1.
The Bayesian approach for selecting the best candidate probability distribution is based
on the Bayes rule and the total probability theorem. Let Z denote the observed data, Оё the
parameters each model, the main steps can be summarized in the following points:
вҖў Evaluation of the posterior distribution from Equation 2.9, i.e learning the parameters
of each candidate model Mi, with computation of the proportionality constant c from
Equation 3.9. The inverse normalizing constant cвҲ’1 is equal to Pr(Z|Mi) which, in
accordance with Equation 2.6, can be understood as the likelihood of each model Mi
given the observed data Z
вҖў Assignment of a prior probability Pr(Mi) to each candidate model. If there is no
preference on a specific model based on prior knowledge, then equal probabilities can
be assigned such as they sum up to 1
вҖў Following the BayesвҖҷ rule the posterior probability of each model Mi can be computed
from,
Pr(Mi|Z) = Pr(Z|Mi)Pr(Mi)
Pr(Z)
,
(3.16)
30
==PAGE:36==
3 Methods Description
where Pr(Z) is computed from the total probability theorem,
Pr(Z) =
m
вҲ‘
i=1
Pr(Z|Mi)Pr(Mi)
(3.17)
The updated-posterior probability Pr(Mi|Z) can be used as a measure for selecting the best
model.
When limited data are available or the statistical uncertainty is high, it can be useful to
combine different probabilistic models for making predictions. Consider again for example
the Mohr-Coulomb soil strength parameters for which different probability distributions have
been reported in the literature. Especially for design parameters and reliability assessment, it
can be beneficial to to combine proposed probability distribution types under the Bayesian
framework. The resulting model would be a mixture of non necessarily normal distributions,
also called Bayesian model fusion (Straub, 2020).
The approach followed in this study for performing a model fusion, can be summarized in
three points:
вҖў Evaluation of the predictive distribution (PDF or CDF) from Equation 3.12 or 3.13 for
each candidate model. The predictive PDF may be written as ЛңfX(x|Mi) to indicate the
dependence on the model Mi
вҖў Calculation of the posterior probability Pr(Mi|Z) of each model following Equation
3.16
вҖў Expression of the final predictive model, as a weighted summation of different probabil-
ity distributions,
ЛңfX(x) =
m
вҲ‘
i=1
Pr(Mi|Z) ЛңfX(x|Mi).
(3.18)
Equation 3.18 indicates that the separate predictions made from each predictive distribution
ЛңfX(x|Mi) are then weighted with the posterior probability Pr(Mi|Z) of each model.
3.3 Joint distribution of dependent random variables
Some theoretical aspects and the reasoning of constructing a multivariate distribution for
modeling dependent uncertain geotechnical parameters were discussed in Section 2.3.2. The
construction of a joint probability distribution can be achieved based on copula functions.
A formal definition of an n-dimensional copula corresponds to a multivariate distribution
over the domain [0, 1]n, with uniform marginal distributions in the closed interval [0, 1]
(Embrechts et al., 2001; Nelsen, 2006).
31
==PAGE:37==

The inverse transformation method is a general method for generating a random variate X from
a distribution FX(x) (e.g. (Ross, 2013)). Consider an one-to-one mapping function X = T(U)
such that if U вҲјU(0, 1), X follows the distribution FX(x). This holds if (Straub, 2014a),
FX(T(u)) = FU(u).
(3.20)
From Equation 3.20 it follows that the transformation is,
T(u) = FвҲ’1
X (u) = x,
(3.21)
where FвҲ’1
X
the inverse CDF of X, which is also known as inverse quantile (Nelsen, 2006) or
inverse percentile function. For a limited number of distributions the inverse CDF FвҲ’1
X
can be
expressed analytically, while in most applications and for most of the known distributions
the inverse CDF is calculated by built-in software algorithms. Transformations of such type
as in Equation 3.21 are called isoprobabilistic transforms. Similarly, FвҲ’1
X (u) is defined the value
such that, FX(x) = u. This means that the distribution FX(x) is uniformly distributed in (0, 1),
or similarly the random variable
U = F(X),
(3.22)
corresponds to a standard uniform variable in (0, 1) (D.-Q. Li and X.-S. Tang, 2015). The
proof of this statement can be derived as follows (Devroy, 1986),
Pr(F(X) вүӨu) = Pr(X вүӨFвҲ’1(u)) = F(FвҲ’1(u)) = u.
(3.23)
A vector of "pseudo"-random standard uniform samples u can be generated with computer
algorithms that are implemented in programming languages, e.g. random.rand with the numpy
library in Python, rand in MATLAB. These functions use Random Number Generators to
produce a sequence of "pseudo"-random numbers Ui that mimic some statistical characteristics
of random numbers, such as the uniformity in distribution type and the randomness, whose
important part is the independence between the generated samples (Ross, 2013). In many
applications, the generated numbers should be tested under certain statistical tests, to check
if they indeed mimic these properties. However, in most reliability applications the "pseudo"-
random numbers obtained from available programming functions can be readily used (Straub,
2014a). For illustration purposes, Figure 3.1 shows the histogram of 1000 generated standard
uniform samples with one custom made version of the Linear Conguential Generator (LCG),
and with the Mersenne Twister algorithm which is the default algorithm in Python. Figure
3.1 allow a visual inspection of the probability density of the generated u samples. It can

Hence, considering that a vector of random standard uniform samples u is available, these
