from many known distribution types FX(x), can be readily generated using built-in software
functions.
3.3.2 Coupling random variables through copulas
Consider now an n-dimensional vector of continuous random variables X = (X1, X2, ..., Xn)T
representing uncertain geotechnical parameters with known marginal cumulative distribution
functions (CDF) FX1, FX2, ..., FXn. The SklarвҖҷs Theorem (Sklar, 2009; Nelsen, 2006), states that
for continuous marginal CDFs FXi = Pr(Xi вүӨxi), i = 1, ..., n there exist a unique n-copula C
such that for all x in R
n where R the extended real line [вҲ’вҲһ, вҲһ], it holds,
FX(x) = C(FX1(x1), FX2(x2), ..., FXn(xn); Оё),
(3.24)
where Оё denote the copula parameters, depending on its type. Considering Equation 3.22 and
the probability transform ui = FX(xi), the joint CDF of X in Equation 3.24 can be written as,
FX(x) = C(u1, u2, ..., un; Оё).
(3.25)
According to the SklarвҖҷs theorem (Nelsen, 2006, pp. 22), for any u in [0, 1]n it also holds,
C(u1, u2, ..., un) = FX(FвҲ’1
X1 (u1), FвҲ’1
X2 (u2), ..., FвҲ’1
Xn (un); Оё).
(3.26)
Hence, the copula function C describes a statistical dependence structure between the random
variables X and links different marginal CDFs FXi to construct their joint CDF FX(x) (Em-
brechts et al., 2001; Joe, 2016; Nelsen, 2006). It is important that copula functions themselves
33
==PAGE:39==
3 Methods Description
are independent of the marginal CDFs FXi i = 1, ...n, and thus they can be separated. This
means that the marginal distributions of various soil properties and a correlation measure
which often is computed based on observed data, suffice to constructing their joint distri-
bution with a copula function. Moreover, Equation 3.26 indicates that with an appropriate
method for generating standard uniform samples (u1, ..., un) from the copula distribution, it
is possible to obtain dependent samples (x1, ..., xn) вҶҗ(FвҲ’1
X1 (u1), ..., FвҲ’1
Xn (un)). The sampling
procedure, however, includes more steps than the described ones in section 3.3.1.
The joint PDF fX of X which exists under conditions similar as in Equation 3.24(see Nelsen,
2006), can be derived following Equation 3.27,
fX(x) = c(FX1(x1), FX2(x2), ..., FXn(xn))
n

i=1
fXi(xi)
(3.27)
where c is the copula density function,
c(u1, ..., cn) = вҲӮnC(u1, ..., un)
вҲӮu1, ..., вҲӮun
(3.28)
3.3.3 Gaussian Copula
As pointed out in Section 2.3.2 there exist different copula functions, such as the t, Frank,
Clayton, Gumbel, and Plackett copulas, that can be used to characterize the dependency
structure between geotechnical parameters. A special copula type is the Gaussian copula,
which belongs to the Elliptical family of copulas (e.g. (Embrechts et al., 2001)). In this case,
Equation 3.26, can be written as (Embrechts et al., 2001),
C(u1, u2, ..., un; R0) = ОҰn(ОҰвҲ’1(u1), ОҰвҲ’1(u2), ..., ОҰвҲ’1(un); R0).
(3.29)
where ОҰn(u; R) the n-dimensional multivariate standard normal CDF, ОҰвҲ’1(ui) the inverse
(marginal) standard normal CDF, ui = FXi(xi) the marginal CDF of Xi and R0 a linear corre-
lation matrix of the multivariate normal distribution underlying the Gaussian copula. The
Gaussian copula operates under the assumption which states that: marginally transformed
random variables to the standard normal space, follow the joint (multivariate) normal dis-
tribution (Straub, 2014a). Thus, the Gaussian copula can be completely defined for known
marginal CDFs and a linear correlation matrix. It should be noted, that there is an impor-
tant distinction between dependence and correlation. Dependence is a more general concept
than correlation. While statistical correlation implies dependence between a set of random
variables, the opposite cannot be stated, i.e. dependence does not imply correlation. For
instance, two variables are not independent if there is no linear correlation between them,
since they can be subjected to a different dependence type. It is worth mentioning that the
Gaussian copula cannot describe tail dependence (e.g. (Nelsen, 2006; Embrechts et al., 2001)),
i.e. dependence in upper-right or lower left quadrant tails of a bivariate distribution. this
concept is related to dependence between extreme values.
The Gaussian copula provides an attractive means to construct joint distributions of corre-
lated random variables, which can be used in many uncertainty quantification applications
34
==PAGE:40==
3 Methods Description
and reliability methods. Furthermore, the Gaussian copula is not limited to bivariate distribu-
tions, but it is applicable to construct joint distributions in higher (n > 2) dimensions. Some
examples of usage of the Gaussian copula include:
вҖў Approximate computation of the Probability of failure, design point and reliability
index with the First Order Reliability Method (FORM). The joint distribution of the
input parameters is needed and the method proceeds by transforming: all (potentially
non-normal) random variables, their joint distribution and the respective limit state
function g(X) to the standard normal space g(U). A non-linear limit state function is
linearized by means of first order Taylor series expansion at the design point which is
computed as the point with highest probability density in the standard normal space.
Hence, the limit state surface g(x) = 0 is approximated by its tangent at the design
point (e.g. (P. L. Liu and Der Kiureghian, 1986; Straub, 2014a)).
вҖў Computation of the failure probability with Monte Carlo simulation. Samples of the
joint probability density are generated from the joint distribution of the variables and
the limit state function is evaluated at each sample.
A linear correlation matrix can be obtained from linear correlation coefficient pairs. In
geotechnical engineering, the most popular measure of linear dependency, i.e. correlation,
is the PearsonвҖҷs rho correlation coefficient. Reasons of its popularity is the computational
simplicity, and the fact that geotechnical literature is full of linear transformation models
between geotechnical parameters, thus the correlation concept is most often related to
this specific measure.
However, it should be noted that the usage of PearsonвҖҷs rho as
measure of correlation is only valid in case of Gaussian copulas, where it is imposed that
the transformed variables are jointly normal. Hence, it can be said, that it is not a copula
based measure of statistical dependence, but it is associated with the underlying normal
distribution of the variables. Furthermore, PearsonвҖҷs rho is just one type of dependence
measures. Other statistical measures that are applicable in more general types of copulas,
are the rank correlation coefficients, SpearmanвҖҷs rho and KendallвҖҷs tau, which can overcome
some weaknesses of the PearsonвҖҷs rho (Embrechts et al., 2001; X. S. Tang et al., 2013; D.-Q. Li
and X.-S. Tang, 2015).
Nevertheless, since the Gaussian copula was preferred in this study due to its aforemen-
tioned advantages, the PearsonвҖҷs rho provides a valid measure of statistical dependence
between the random variables. The range of a PearsonвҖҷs correlation coefficient ПҒij, between
a pair of variables is bounded in [ПҒmin, ПҒmax] вҠӮ[вҲ’1, 1]. The PearsonвҖҷs correlation coefficient
between a pair of variables can be defined as,
ПҒij = Cov(Xi, Xj)
ПғXiПғXj

and Cov(Xi, Xj) the covariance of the variables Xi, Xj. By definition of the expectation (e.g.

3.3.4 The Nataf model
Random variables whose dependency can be described by a Gaussian copula, are said to have
the Nataf distribution and the Nataf model can be used to construct the multivariate distribution
(Nataf, 1962; P. L. Liu and Der Kiureghian, 1986; X. S. Tang et al., 2013; Straub, 2014a).
Consider a vector of random variables X = (X1, X2, ..., Xn)T with known marginal CDFs
FXi(xi), i = 1, ..., n. The Nataf transformation can be used to transform the correlated random
variables X from their physical space, to uncorrelated and consequently, independent normal
random variables Z in the standard normal space. The Nataf transformation T : X вҶ’Z, is
constituted by two functions T = T2 в—ҰT1. The main steps are described in the following (ERA
Group, 2020):
1. Transformation of the correlated random variables X to correlated standard normal
variables bZ вҲјN(0, R0) (where 0 the n-dimensional null vector):
T1 : Xi вҶ’ЛҶZi = ОҰвҲ’1 (FXi(xi)) , i = 1, ..., n
(3.33)
where ОҰвҲ’1 the inverse standard normal CDF (recall from Equation 3.22 Ui = FXi(xi) вҲј
U(0, 1)). The correlation of transformed to the standard normal space variables bZ, is
described by the correlation matrix R0.
2. Transformation of the correlated standard normal variables bZ, to uncorrelated (and
independent) standard normal variables Z вҲјN(0, I) where I the n-dimensional identity
matrix:
T2 : bZ вҶ’Z = О“bZ,
(3.34)
where О“ denotes the inverse Cholesky factor of R0. The latter contains the correlation
coefficients ПҒ0,ij between the transformed standard normal random variables bZ, which
are in general not the same as in the input correlation matrix R, e.g. estimated sample
Pearson rho. The Cholesky factor О“ is obtained as the inverse of the lower triangle matrix
36
==PAGE:42==
3 Methods Description
(i.e. all elements in the upper triangle equal to 0) L that satisfies the matrix Equation
3.35
LLT = R0.
(3.35)
Equation 3.35 refers to the Choleski decomposition of the correlation matrix R0. The
Cholesky factorization can be performed with software functions. For instance, in
Python with use of the scipy library (www-03 ),
1 L = scipy.linalg.cholesky(R_0,lower=True)
It should be noted, that the Choleski decomposition and the matrix inversion can be
accomplished only if the correlation matrix is positive-definite and this is a requirement
for constructing a valid multivariate distribution (e.g. (Strang, 2006; Ross, 2013; Lebrun
and Dutfoy, 2009a). The correlation coefficients in the matrices R0 and R are related
through Equation 3.36 (ERA Group, 2020),

(3.36)
where П•2 is the bivariate standard normal PDF with correlation coefficients ПҒ0,ij. For
given correlation coefficient ПҒij, the computation of the correlation coefficient ПҒ0,ij
through Equation 3.36 can be performed iteratively to obtain ПҒ0,ij. The method which
was applied in this study, is based on a two-dimensional Gauss-Legendre quadrature
integration (ERA Group, 2020).

The distribution model is generally referred as the Nataf distribution.
To simulate dependent samples from the joint distribution of the random variables X
to their physical space, the inverse Nataf transformation is required. The inverse transform

The realistic example presented in this section, focused on explaining the procedure for
constructing a joint-bivariate PDF of variables that have the lognormal distribution as marginal.
A sampling method from the joint distribution, was presented as well. The extension to more
dimensions when the variables are normal, lognormal or a combination thereof, is more or
44
==PAGE:50==
3 Methods Description
less straightforward. However, geotechnical parameters in general, are not always lognormal
distributed, even if the lognormal distribution is one of the most commonly used models
(see section 3.4.1). It should be noted that analytical expressions for the transformations
are available in limited cases. Moreover, the correlation coefficients between the standard
normal variables were here computed analytically from Equation 3.42), due to the underlying
relation with the normal distribution. When other types of marginals are involved, numerical
computation is unavoidable. An efficient and convenient computational tool for performing
the transformations and constructing joint PDFs of multiple random variables with different
types of marginals, is the ERANataf class of functions, provided in the homepage of the ERA
Group of TUM (ERA Group, 2020).
In the following section, the construction of a multivariate distribution, based on the
JohnsonвҖҷs system of distributions is discussed.
3.4 Constructing a Multivariate distribution with JohnsonвҖҷs
marginal distributions
3.4.1 The JohnsonвҖҷs system
Most geotechnical parameters, such as soil shear strengths, can take only non-negative
values. Moreover, soil physics define some boundaries in the values that can be assigned
to a specific soil property related to a specific soil type, or to some other conditions. For
example OCR in overconsolidated clays takes values larger than 1. Considering such physical
bounds, a normal distribution may be not an appropriate probabilistic model for most soil
properties, since normal random variables are unbounded and can take any value in R. For
this reason, the lognormal distribution is a more suitable model for geotechnical properties
and it is often preferred in statistical analyses, since it can overcome some problems associated
with modeling non-normal real data, while retaining beneficial properties of the normal
distribution. The lognormal distribution is bounded at 0. Moreover, a lognormal distributed
random variable Y is translated to a normal random variable by with the logarithmic transform
ln Y, and as discussed in sections 3.1.3, 3.3.5.
K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2013 adopted a more flexible type of marginals, called JohnsonвҖҷs
system of distributions, whose one special member is the shifted lognormal distribution
(lower bound > 0). These distributions can describe a wide range of distribution shapes
and they can be proper choices for bounded soil parameters. The approach to construct
the multivariate model is again based on Nataf transformations (Ching, Lin, et al., 2017),
meaning that the underlying distribution of transformed variables to normal space would
be the multi-Normal distribution. The JohnsonвҖҷs system is a convenient choice for marginal
CDFs because there exist analytical expressions for the transformation to the standard normal
space and its inverse. Such transformations, were presented in the previous section. To retain
the authors mathematical notations in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014), let X denote a standard
normal variable and Y a non-normal random variable. Then the (marginal) transform (recall
45
==PAGE:51==
3 Methods Description
Equation 3.33) to the standard normal space writes,
Xi = ОҰвҲ’1[Fi(Yi)],
(3.50)
and the inverse, i.e. from standard normal space to the physical space of the soil property,
Yi = FвҲ’1
i
[ОҰ(Xi)],
(3.51)
where Fi the CDF of Yi, FвҲ’1
i
the inverse CDF, ОҰ the CDF of the standard normal variable.
The JohnsonвҖҷs system (Johnson, 1949) consists of three members, namely SU, SB and
SL. The former member is associated with a three parameter lognormal distribution. Let
aX, bX, aY, bY denote the distribution parameters (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014). A closed
form expression for the transformation to the standard normal space, given by (Slifker and
Shapiro, 1980), writes as follows,
X вҲ’bX

The parameters bX, aX define the shape of the distribution, while bY, aY are location and
scale parameters, respectively. Unique distributions can be derived for a large number of
combinations of mean, standard deviation, skewness, and kurtosis that may characterize
a given set of observed data. In addition, the distribution type remains unchanged after
conditioning when the transformations 3.52 are used to translate a non-normal random
variable to the standard normal space. The reader is referred to (K. K. Phoon and Ching,
2015, pp. 45), (K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2013) for more details on the three members of the
JohnsonвҖҷs system.

3.4.2 JohnsonвҖҷs member selection and estimation of distribution parameters
(Slifker and Shapiro, 1980) proposed a computationally convenient approach to select the
distribution type and estimate the parameters of each member. The distribution type selection
is based on percentiles and the main steps of the method can be summarized in 5 steps (see
Slifker and Shapiro, 1980, pp. 240):
1. Choose a number z > 0. Based on (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014) and (Slifker and
Shapiro, 1980) an appropriate choice can be z = 0.7
2. Compute 4 percentiles of the standard normal distribution ОҰ, corresponding to -3z, -z, z,
3z. From tables of the standard normal distribution, or with simple software functions,
the four percentiles correspond to:
pa = ОҰ(вҲ’2.1) = 0.018
pb = ОҰ(вҲ’0.7) = 0.242
pc = ОҰ(0.7) = 0.758
pd = ОҰ(2.1) = 0.982
The validity of the chosen value of z, can be checked with an empirical rule, stating that
the sample size in the database should be N вүҘ10/pa = 556, for z = 0.7. If this is not
satisfied the z value should be reduced (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014)
3. Calculate the Y values associated with the above percentiles: ya = FвҲ’1(pa), yb = FвҲ’1(pb),
yc = FвҲ’1(pc) and yd = FвҲ’1(pd). However since the CDF of Y is not known, these values
may be calculated directly from the data in the generic database, i.e. yi = pi sample
percentile. This computation is trivial with the use of a software function. For instance,
using the numpy library in Python
1 y_a, y_b, y_c, y_d = numpy.quantile(Y, (p_a, p_b, p_c, p_d))
4. From the ya, yb, yc, yd values, compute three parameters:
m = yd вҲ’yc
n = yb вҲ’ya
p = yc вҲ’yb
(3.56)
5. In the last step, let D = mn/p2 and select the JohnsonвҖҷs distribution type as:
SU, if D > 1
SB, if D < 1
SL, if D = 1
(3.57)

3.4.3 Hypothesis testing: The Kolmogorov-Smirnov test
The normality of the distribution of the transformed random variable X can be visually
checked buy plotting it against the reference standard normal distribution. Moreover, sta-
tistical tests can be performed to check if indeed is standard normal.
A popular and
48
==PAGE:54==
3 Methods Description
flexible nonparametric statistical test for checking the goodness-of-fit of an empirical distribu-
tion constructed from the available samples, and a theoretical distribution, is the so called
Kolmogorov-Smirnov (KS) test (e.g. (Conover, 1999; Ross, 2013)). While other statistical tests
are also available for this task (e.g. Shapiro-Wilk test for normality), the KS test is adopted in
(Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015), and this was performed in
the current study, as well.
The K-S test provides a statistic that can be used to accept or reject the null hypothesis H0,
i.e.
H0 : X has the normal distribution
(3.62)
The K-S statistic Dn is asymptotically distributed (n вҶ’вҲһ) as the Kolmogorov distribution,
Dn = вҲҡ
n sup
x
|Fn(x) вҲ’F(x)| = вҲҡ
n sup
x
Fn(x) вҲ’ОҰ
 x вҲ’Вө
Пғ
 ,
(3.63)
where n the number of samples and sup the supremum of the set distances. Dn measures
of the absolute difference between the theoretical and empirical distributions. Hence, small
Dn values lead to acceptance of the null hypothesis H0, while for large Dn values the null
hypothesis should be rejected. Consider a critical value c such that if,
Dn > c вҮ’Reject H0
Dn вүӨc вҮ’Accept H0
(3.64)
A threshold value c can be chosen such that the probability of rejection of H0, i.e. Pr(Dn > c),
is equal to a small quantity a (normally a = 0.05), which corresponds to the significance
level of the KS test. The c value corresponds to the (1 вҲ’a) percentile of the Kolmogorov
distribution (e.g. (Marsaglia et al., 2003)).
Let pvalue denote the probability Pr(Dn вүҘdn), where dn the realizations of Dn, then H0 is
rejected if p < a. Software functions can be used for efficient computation of the probability
Pr(Dn вүҘdn. For instance, to implement the KS test for a standardized X variable, and derive
the Dn statistic and pvalue in Python, the following function implemented with the scipy library
(www-03 ) can be used,
1 D_n, p_value = scipy.stats.kstest(rvs=X, cdf=вҖҷnormвҖҷ)
3.4.4 The multivariate distribution
It should be noted, that even if the marginally transformed to the standard normal space
random variables X, follow indeed the normal distribution, this does not imply that collec-
tively are multi-Normal distributed (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching, K. K. Phoon,
Khan, et al., 2020; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019). However, the multivariate normality is
again the key assumption made in this method (see also section 3.3.3). Hence, after defining
the marginal distributions, i.e. the JohnsonвҖҷs distributions of the uncertain soil parameters
Y and the transformed to the standard normal space standard normal CDFs of X, the next
step towards the complete definition of a multi-Normal distribution is the computation of a
49
==PAGE:55==
3 Methods Description
linear (Pearson) correlation matrix. This correlation matrix is composed from the correlation
coefficient pairs between the variables Xi and Xj. The computation can be performed based on
Equation 3.30. Let ПҒij denote the correlation coefficient, in case of standard normal variables
one can define ПҒij as (second moment method),
ПҒij = 1
n
n
вҲ‘
k=1
Xi,kXj,k,
(3.65)
where n the number of pairwise (Xi, Xj) and Xi,k the kth sample in the Xi dataset.
The bootstrap technique
The pairwise correlation coefficients between the original variables (Yi, Yj) are generally not
the same with the transformed (Xi, Xj) as highlighted in section 3.3.3. Moreover, the statistical
uncertainty can be high if the estimation of the correlation coefficients between (Xi, Xj) is
based on the moment method of Equation 3.65. Some reasons for high statistical uncertainty
could be the presence of bad quality data in the database, the combination of data from many
different sites and the low amount of data-pairs in the dataset. In addition, it is unlikely that
the ПҒij components will lead to a "positive-definite" matrix, which is critical for constructing
the multi-Normal distribution of X (e.g. Straub, 2020, pp.136).
The authors in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014) adopted a sampling replacement technique
(Efron and Tibshirani, 1993), called Bootstrap to obtain a large number of Оҙij estimates, to
address cases where significant statistical uncertainty is present. The method proceeds by
sampling (with replacement) n random pairs of (Xi, Xj) from the observed pairs in the dataset,
and the correlation coefficient is estimated based on the resampled data. When the process
is repeated many times, the empirical distribution can be constructed from the coefficient
samples and this can be used to approximate the possible range of each ПҒij coefficient, through
the computation of a confidence interval (e.g. 90% CI in Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014).
However, the resampling technique, does not guarantee positive-definiteness of the resulting
correlation matrix.
This problem can be possibly addressed by introducing a positive-
definiteness check for the correlation matrix, that results after each round of ПҒij estimations,
and by repeating the steps until a desired number of valid correlation matrices is accepted.
For n number of soil parameters in the dataset, there exist n(n вҲ’1)/2 possible pairwise
correlations. The proposed approach in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014) is implemented
through the following steps:
1. For each of the n(n вҲ’1)/2 pairwise correlations (Xi, Xj):
вҖў Generate a sample (Xi, Xj) with replacement from the observed data pairs. (For
example, the method numpy.random.Generator.choice in numpy library in Python
can be used (www-02 ))
вҖў Compute the correlation coefficient ПҒij from Equation 3.65
2. Check if the resulting correlation matrix R is positive-definite. Positive-definiteness
implies that all eigenvalues of the matrix О»R, are larger than 0: О»R > 0 (Strang, 2006).
50
==PAGE:56==
3 Methods Description
Accept and store the sampled correlation matrix if it is positive-definite (e.g. in Python
with numpy: (numpy.linalg.eigvals(Csample) > 0).all()), otherwise reject.
3. Repeat steps 1-2 until a desired number k of correlation matrices have been obtained.
The final correlation matrix can be calculated as the mean value of k correlation matrices.
Having computed a valid correlation matrix from the available generic database, the
multivariate normal PDF of X = (X1, X2, ..., Xm) is defined as,
fX(x) =
1
(2ПҖ)m/2p
|C|
exp

вҲ’1
2(x вҲ’Вө)TCвҲ’1(x вҲ’Вө)

(3.66)
where |C| denotes the determinant of the covariance matrix C, Вө the mean vector and m the
number of random variables. The covariance matrix C is constructed as (Straub, 2020),
C = DXRXXDX
(3.67)
where DX = diag[Пғ1, Пғ2, ..., Пғm] the diagonal matrix containing the standard deviations of the
variables. If all variables in X are standard normal variables, then Вө = 0 and the covariance
and correlation matrices are identical, i.e. C = RXX.
Simulation
Simulation of correlated standard normal random variables X can be performed in the same
way as discussed in section 3.3.4 and Equations 3.39. Let Z denote a vector of independent
standard normal samples, and L the Cholesky factor, such that LLT = C, where C is identical
to the m Г— m correlation matrix. The transformation to correlated standard normal variables
writes X = LZ (note that in Equation 3.39 the correlated variables are denoted by bZ). Finally,
correlated random variables Y in the original/physical space can be obtained from Equation
3.51 for each JohnsonвҖҷs member (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014 pp. 12 or Ching, Lin, et al.,
2017, pp.1209),
Y =

3.5 Bayesian framework for Multivariate distributions
3.5.1 The multivariate distribution conditioning
Up to this point the proposed method for constructing the multivariate PDF (Ching and
K. K. Phoon, 2014), is based on the same principles discussed in section 3.3.4, i.e. the
multivariate PDF is a Nataf (P. L. Liu and Der Kiureghian, 1986) (or translation) PDF. The
exception is that the multivariate distribution is constructed based on the flexible JohnsonвҖҷs
distributions, for which there exist analytical expression of their inverse CDF, which facilitate
the transformation to the original space of the variables as shown in Equation 3.68. An
important motivation for adopting the JohnsonвҖҷs system in constructing the multivariate PDF
is associated with the conditioning of the distribution. The marginal JohnsonвҖҷs distributions
retain their type when conditioned to some investigation data, and only parameters are
updated (Ching, Lin, et al., 2017; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; K. K. Phoon and Ching,
2015). Hence, if XвҖІ the updated normal distributed variable with parameters aвҖІ
X, bвҖІ

This is a more realistic scenario, since during a site investigation there may have been

update these properties based on the available test indices, and extract some information
about their dependence at the specific location of the site.

The joint conditional distribution contains more information than the traditional pairwise
regression used in common geotechnical practice. The entire distribution of the soil property,
or the entire multivariate distribution of a set of properties, is updated based on a set of
other (potentially) dependent parameters, and not only single values of two parameters. Only
one observation per variable, i.e measurements at a specific depth, was considered in this
simplified example.
In the following sections, the procedure of constructing a Site-specific multivariate distribu-
tion based on the proposed approach of (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) will be discussed.
The advantages of the Bayesian framework are further exploited. An effective method for
hybridizing a multivariate distribution based on generic data acting as prior, and an imprecise
distribution constructed from multiple, but limited site data is presented. The resulting
posterior ore quasi-site-specific model, can be used for enhanced site-specific predictions.
3.5.2 Conjugate priors
The Bayesian framework, and its main components: i) prior distribution, likelihood function,
posterior distribution, was first introduced in section 2.3.1, and a more detailed description
for the univariate distribution updating based on data, was given in section 3.1.3. In section
2.3.1, it was explained that in certain cases, there exist a type of distributions, called conjugate
priors whose adoption as priors, can lead to a posterior distribution of the same type with the
distribution used to model a random variable. The concept of conjugate priors is applicable
in the multivariate case, and such distributions can substantially reduce the computational
effort in multidimensional problems. In fact, conjugate priors exist under the assumption
of joint normality, and their adoption ensures that the posterior multivariate model retains
its distribution type, i.e. they lead to a multi-Normal posterior distribution. Numerical
integration techniques are not effective for evaluating the posterior distribution when many
59
==PAGE:65==
3 Methods Description
unknown parameters are involved, and advanced sampling algorithms are adopted for this
scope. Conjugate priors allow exact sampling from the joint posterior distribution.
Recall the multi-Normal PDF given in Equation 3.66. Consider a collection of multivariate
data included in the m Г— n matrix X, where m the number of rows and n the number of
random variables. The data are transformed from Y-space to X-space discussed in the
previous sections. Consider also that the data-set is complete, i.e. no missing entries. The
observations of the random variables, are described by the likelihood function (recall Equation
2.6 for single measurement). The PDF of xj, i = 1, 2, ..., m for each set of measurements at a
specific depth, given the distribution parameters Вө and covariance C writes,
fX(xj|Вө, C) = N(xj|Вө, C).
(3.79)
where xT
j is the 1 Г— m vector containing measurements of (X1, X2, ..., Xm), i.e. the j вҲ’th row
of the matrix X. Assuming independence between measurements at different locations, the
total likelihood for m number of records writes,
f (X|Вө, C) =
m
вҲҸ
j=1
N(xj|Вө, C) = |C|вҲ’m/2(2ПҖ)(вҲ’mВ·n)/2 exp

Before any site investigation, the statistical uncertainty in the parameters Вө and C is
characterized by the prior PDFs f (Вө) and f (C), which reflect the prior knowledge. By
applying Bayesian Analysis, these distributions are updated into posterior PDFs, based on
the observed data, i.e. f (Вө|Xo) and f (C|Xo). Conjugate priors facilitate the selection of non-
informative priors. It is desirable to adopt conjugate priors and choose their parameters such
as they become non-informative. The conjugate prior PDF of Вө is defined as the multivariate
normal distribution (Gelman et al., 2013; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019),
f (Вө) = N(Вө|Вө0, C0)
(3.81)
where in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) (Вө0, C0) denote the prior mean vector and the prior
covariance matrix, respectively. A non-informative prior can be defined from Equation 3.81
with the choice of Вө0 as the null vector and a diagonal C0 matrix with very high values. The
prior PDF of C corresponds to the inverse Wishart distribution (Gelman et al., 2013),
f (C) = IW(C|ОЈ, v) =

3.5.3 The Gibbs Sampler
An efficient stochastic simulation method for tackling problems that involve a large number
of parameters, is the so called Gibbs Sampler (S. Geman and D. Geman, 1984; Gilks et al.,
1996; Ching, Muto, et al., 2006). The Gibbs sampler belongs to the Markov-Chain Monte
Carlo (MCMC) algorithms, and is considered as a special case of the Metropolis-Hastings
algorithm (Gelman et al., 2013). The Gibbs sampling technique can be used to indirectly
generate random samples from the joint posterior distribution of the parameters, given that
it is possible to sample directly from each of the full conditional distributions. The method
is based on the decomposition of the unknown parameters into groups, such that direct
sampling of one group can be achieved conditional on the other groups. By sequentially
sampling from the conditional distribution of each group given the other groups, it can be
proved that the generated samples asymptotically converge to the desired distribution (Ching
and K. K. Phoon, 2019) (as n вҲ’вҶ’вҲһ, where n the number of repetitions).
The combination of the Gibbs sampler with conjugate priors, reduces significantly the
computational effort, and allows exact sampling from the distribution of each group given
the remaining groups of parameters, since the conditional distributions can be expressed in
an analytical manner. More specifically, the conditional PDFs of the parameters retain their
prior form and they can be written as,
f (Вө|X, c, a) = N

Hence, Gibbs sampling is an appropriate method for handling missing data. Typically, the
implementation of the algorithm involves a considerable number of generated samples that
have to been discarded. These samples are generated in an initial burn-in number of iterations
(typically in the order of 103 number of iterations), and they do not follow the required
posterior distribution (e.g. for "burn-in" period in MCMC, see Straub and Papaioannou,
61
==PAGE:67==
3 Methods Description
2015 pp. 247). Worth mentioning, is also that the Gibbs sampler does not reject samples,
unlike other MCMC algorithms.
Recall that if there were no missing entries in X and if the measurements at different
depths are assumed independent, then the multivariate PDF of X is given from Equation
3.80 Consider now an incomplete collection of multivariate data included in the m Г— n matrix

vector Вө and the covariance matrix C are partioned similarly as was shown in the examples
of section 3.5.1. The conditional distribution of xj,u given xj,o is the multivariate normal

The proposed implementation of the Gibbs sampling algorithm in (Ching and K. K. Phoon,
2019) for generating samples from the required joint distribution, can be summarized in the
following iterative steps:
1. Initialization of (Вө, C, a, Xu) at arbitrary values. Note that Xu = x1,u, ..., xm,u, i.e. all
missing entries in X, are intialized so that the matrix X does not contain missing values.
2. Generate sample Вө вҲјf (Вө|X, c, a), given in Equation 3.85. In Python, using functions
from the NumPy and Scipy libraries this can be programmed as follows:
1 import numpy as np
2 from scipy.linalg import inv
3 from scipy.stats import multivariate_normal as mvn
4 mean_vector = inv(inv(C_0) + m * inv(C)) @ np.sum(inv(C) @ X, axis=0)
5 C_matrix = inv(inv(C_0) + m * inv(C))
6 mu = mvn.rvs(mean=mean_vector, cov=C_matrix)

ОЈ = diag(4/a1, 4/a2, ..., 4/an):
1 from scipy.stats import invwishart

# degrees of freedom
4 C = invwishart.rvs(scale = Sigma_matrix, df = v)

As mentioned earlier, a number of initial generated samples should be discarded. The
"burn-in" period can be visually determined, e.g. with trace plots, autocorrelation plots. The
collected samples after the burn-in period follow the posterior PDFs f (Вө, C, a, Xu|Xo), where
boldsymbolXo = (x1,o, ..., xm,o) the observed (training) data.
3.5.4 The predictive distribution
Recall the definition of the predictive distribution given in section 3.1.4, where univariate
distributions were considered. The posterior distribution characterizes the uncertainty in
the distribution parameters. The predictive distribution characterizes the uncertainty of the
random variables, and is the distribution of x conditional on the observations Xo, at each
spatial location, i.e. f (x|Xo). Two scenarios are proposed in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019;
Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020). In (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) it is considered
that the database is global and the target site is local. In (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al.,
2020) the scenario considers that the database is municipal and the site is local. These scenarios
are based on the following:
вҖў The global-local scenario in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) considers that the mean
vector and the covariance matrix of the database and of the site are different. Hence, two
different sets of parameters (Вө, C) need to be determined. Let (Вөdb, Cdb denote the mean
and covariance matrix of the database. Based on the assumption that the large number
of observations for each soil property in the global database, suffices to characterize the
statistical uncertainty in the distribution parameters, they define the database predictive
PDF as a standard normal multivariate PDF f (x|Xdb,o) = N(x|0, Cdb) (Ching and K. K.
Phoon, 2019, pp. 7). On the other hand, the statistical uncertainty in (Вөs, Cs), i.e. the
parameters of the site-specific PDF, can be high due to limited amount of data. Hence,
these are inferred from the site-specific data through Bayesian Analysis using the Gibbs
Sampler. The site-specific predictive PDF in this case, derives from the Bayes rule and
the total probability theorem, and it can be expressed as mixture of multivariate normal
PDFs.
63
==PAGE:69==
3 Methods Description
вҖў The municipal-local scenario in (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020) assumes that
the municipal database and the target site follow the same (Вө, C). Due to lower amount
of data in a typical municipal database, the distribution parameters are inferred with
Bayesian Analysis and the Gibbs Sampler. Hence, the generic (municipal) database
predictive distribution f (x|Xdb,o), is not a standard normal multivariate distribution in
this case, and this is constructed using the Bayes rule and the total probability theorem.
The two approaches follow different methodologies for the construction of a site-specific
model that is able to make predictions at site scale. The constructed predictive PDF based
on limited, sparse site data in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019), cannot provide reasonable
predictions for the value of a soil parameter at an unobserved depth. Hence, the authors
propose a hybridization of the site-specific predictive PDF and the generic (global) database
predictive PDF, under a slightly modified Bayesian framework, to produce a quasi-site-specific
model.
On the other hand, since the municipal database and the site are considered to follow the
same (Вө, C) in (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), the authors propose the construction
of a quasi-site-specific model by further applying Bayesian updating to the database predictive
PDF f (x|Xdb,o), given the site observed data Xs,o, i.e. f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o).
In the present study, a regional database was considered, and the site under investigation
is local. For this scenario, both cases were considered. Note that the Bayesian updating is
only applicable if the target site is in the coverage of the regional database, which applies in
the present study. The Hybrid approach is applicable even when the site data are not in the
regional database coverage. To test this assumption simple correlation plots (site and regional
data) can be used. Hence,
вҖў The regional predictive PDF f (x|Xdb,o) was constructed on based on the observed
data of another sub-dataset extracted from the regional database. In this case, the
construction of a quasi-Site-specific model was based on the updating of the regional
predictive distribution, using the site data. The statistical uncertainty in the regional
(Вөdb,t, Cdb,t) can be high due to the lower number of observations in this dataset. Thus,
Bayesian analysis with use of the Gibbs Sampler was adopted for the evaluation of
the regional database PDF parameters, and subsequently to construct the predictive
regional PDF as a mixture of multivariate normal distributions. The regional predictive
PDF was then updated based on the site data, to derive the quasi-site-specific model.
The assumption that the site data are located in the coverage of the regional database
was tested, meaning that they can follow the same parameters (Вөdb,t, Cdb,t).
вҖў The hybridization approach proposed in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) was adopted to
construct a quasi-site-specific model for making predictions at site level. In this method
the Site-Specific PDF is assumed not to follow the same parameters (mean, covariance)
with the regional PDF. Hence, the Gibbs Sampler is again performed to obtain posterior
parametersвҖҷs samples, and construct the site-specific predictive PDF f (x|Xs,o) based on
limited site observations. This distribution is the multiplied with the regional predictive
PDF to produce the hybrid or "quasi-site-specific" model.
64
==PAGE:70==
3 Methods Description
3.5.5 Predictive PDF of the generic database
In the following, the procedure for evaluating the predictive PDF f (x|Xo) based on the
observed data is discussed. Consider that the GS algorithm is implemented using the data of
the generic database Xdb. Let Xdb,o and Xdb,u denote the observed and the unobserved entries
in the generic database. Let (Вөdb,t, Cdb,t, Xdb,u,t) denote the collected samples at time step t,
after the burn-in period T. The samples are distributed as the posterior PDF f (Вөdb, Cdb|Xdb,o).
Following the BayesвҖҷ rule and the total probability theorem, the database predictive PDF
f (x|Xdb,o) can be approximated as a mixture of multi-Normal PDFs,
f (x|Xdb,o) =
Z

1. Draw random sample t index among (tb + 1, tb + 2, ..., T)
2. Sample xnew вҲјN(xnew|Вөdb,t, Cdb,t)
The statistical uncertainty due to limited training data in the database is characterized by the
samples xnew. In Python these steps can be programmed as follows
1 import numpy as np

The regional predictive PDF f (x|Xdb,o) can be updated based on the observed site data, i.e.
(x|Xdb,o,Xs,o). The conditioning process is based again on the total probability theorem, and
the resulting distribution is a mixture of multivariate conditional PDFs f (x|Вөdb,t, Cdb,t, Xs,o),
with non-uniform weights. The reader is referred to (Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020)
for the evaluation details.

3.5.7 Hybridization of Site-specific and Generic Multivariate PDFs
The hybridization concept proposed by (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019), is similar to the
Bayesian framework. The generic predictive database PDF, i.e. f (x|Xdb,o, serves as prior
distribution. Bayesian updating is performed by multiplying the likelihood function with the
prior generic PDF to produce the posterior PDF. The hybridization method is performed by
multiplying the prior generic PDF f (x|Xdb,o), with the site-specific predictive PDF f (x|Xs,o),
to produce a hybrid predictive PDF f (x|hb), i.e.
f (x|hb) вҲқf (x|Xdb,o) Г— f (x|Xs,o).
(3.94)
In (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019) the database predictive PDF is equivalent to the posterior
PDF f (x|Вөdb, Cdb = N(x|0, Cdb) for the reasons that were discussed in section 3.5.4.
A
visualization example of how the hybridization works, is given in (Ching and K. K. Phoon,
2019, pp.8). The hybridization approach is based on the following:
вҖў Preservation of the "uniqueness" of the site. This means that the generic and site-specific
PDF can have different means and covariances
вҖў When the site-specific data-set is sufficiently large, the predictions will mainly depend
on the site-specific PDF
вҖў When the site-specific data are sparse, the predictive PDF will approach the generic
PDF.
The procedure for deriving the hybrid PDF as a mixture of multivariate normal PDFs, is
given in (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019).
Define,
f (x|hb) вҲқf (x|Вөdb, Cdb) В· f (x|Xs,o)

4 Database overview and Elementary data
Analysis
In this chapter, an overview of the constructed regional database is given. The database is
comprised from a large number of sites in the region, and includes measured properties
and derived soil parameters, specifically from results of laboratory tests. The data collection
spans over a 12-year period. A relational SQL database was constructed from Excel spread-
sheets containing measurements on the soil properties, using an open-source platform and
employing SQL and Python programming languages. The SQL database provided extensive
functionalities in terms of handling and retrieving data of interest. Examples of how to
interact with the SQL database, for retrieving data and storing them to be used in calculations,
are included in python functions in the accompanying material of this document.
The Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion was applied to derive the soil strength parameters,
while elasticity moduli were computed from stress-strain curves. Data from Sieve Analysis
and Atterberg limits tests were combined to perform soil classification of the tested specimens,
based on - relevant to the objective of the study - methods (USCS). The vast majority of the
classified soils referred to clayey soils. It was decided to base the further analysis on clay
mixtures. The following section provides an overview of the considered soil parameters in
the constructed regional database.
4.1 Regional Database overview
A relational SQL database was utilized as the basic data-source for compiling a regional
database under MUSIC characteristics. The TUM Geotechnics SQL database is a product
of data analysis on labolaratory tests, and digitization projects that were carried out during
the academic year 2020-21. Documents with detailed description of how the SQL database
was constructed, the SCHEMA and the Tables structures, containing the testsвҖҷ results, are not
included in the present document. These can become available to interested readers, upon
request. Some essential information on the total number of records included in the TUM Geo
Database is visually presented in the following.
The main sources of data in the database are the following laboratory tests:
1. Sieve Analysis tests
2. Plasticity tests
3. Triaxial compression tests and
70
==PAGE:76==
4 Database overview and Elementary data Analysis
4. Unconfined compression tests
Each of the tests corresponds to a SCHEMA structure in the SQL database.
Consider
that each database SCHEMA contains a number of Tables with measurements (e.g. stress-
strain incremental measurements), and a number of derived soil properties (e.g. cohesion,
friction angle). The records (rows) contained in the different Tables, can be linked using the
characteristic "probe-number" (a table column) assigned to each soil specimen.
Figure 4.1 provides a simplified overview of the types of laboratory tests, and the number
of observations, related to the tested specimens numbers ("probenumbers"), that are contained
in the database. On the lower left corner of Figure 4.1 a Venn diagram illustrating the number
of intersections between the different test types, is given. The ellipses correspond to the
tests. The numbers refer to the number of unique specimens, tested in each test. The shaded
parts correspond to the intersections between the various tests, i.e. the number of specimens
tested in multiple types of tests. It is trivial to read the Venn diagram, which is based on the
set theory. For instance, one can observe that from a total 321 number of unique probes in
triaxial tests (see figure on the left, or add numbers on both shaded, and not shaded areas
of TUM_triaxial), 104 of them do not match to any of the other tests. For 84+14=98 of the
triaxial tested specimens, there is available information from both sieve tests and plasticity
tests, which can be used for classification (USCS). On the other hand, there is available
information from Sieve analysis and plasticity tests, for a total of 151 soil specimens, tested in
unconfined compression tests (UC). It can be noticed, that full information is available only
for 14 specimens, i.e. specimens tested in every of the 4 test types.
Additional data that became available during the analysis process (Excel spreadsheets),
and which were not included in the SQL database, were incorporated as well, again based on
the characteristic "probenumber" of each soil specimen. The Venn diagram illustrated on the
right corner of Figure 4.1 provides an overview of the total tested probes, and consequently
the number of rows in the data-set, and of the intersections-matches between the test indices,
after incorporating the additional data. The interpretation follows the same logic.
An incomplete data-set was compiled based on the probe-numbersвҖҷ matches. The "merging"
of the different data-sets was not a trivial task. The main concern was to retain as much
information as possible, on soil strength parameters, e.g. friction angle, undrained shear
strength, while including valuable information from the other test indices. SQL queries
and Python programming were utilized for this scope (some of them can be found in the
supplementary material).
4.1.1 Classification
The USCS (Unified Soil Classification System) (ASTM D2487 - 17e1: 2017) was selected as the
most relevant approach, for the scope of this project. According to this system the soils are
divided in gravel, sand, silt and clay by the grain size limits shown in table 4.1. According to
the unified system, the symbols shown in table 4.2 are used for the classification of coarse
and fine-grained soils. The properties used for the classification are the following:
вҖў grain size limits, see Table 4.1

The classification results related to the entire TUM Geo database specimens, are provided
in Figure 4.2, Figure 4.3 and Table 4.3. In a nutshell:
вҖў A total number of 1677 soil specimens were classified. Main soil classes and detailed
soil description included
вҖў Clayey soils of low plasticity (CL) is the most frequent soil category, with 949 specimens
belonging in this class, as can be seen in Figure 4.2. Clay soils of high plasticity (CH) is
the second most frequent class with 276 total counts. These two classes account for
вүғ73% of the total classified specimens. Fine-grained soils account for the majority
of the classified soils, although a considerable number of specimens are associated to
sandy soils (SC) and gravely soils with sand.
вҖў Table 4.3 provides a more detailed overview of the classified soil specimens. CL, lean
clay is the most frequent detailed soil type, among the classified soils. On the other
hand,
вҖў In this study, the further analysis considered the following main soil categories (plastic
soils):
- clay mixtures: CL, CH;
- silty soils MH, ML (and double CL-ML)
Hence, sands and gravelly soils were excluded.
вҖў Figure 4.3 shows the scatter of the LL, PI data points, plotted on the plasticity chart, used
for the fines-grained soils separation (e.g. see Kolymbas, 2011).

4.1.2 Descriptive statistics
In the following, information and useful descriptive statistics are provided for cohesive,
fine-grained soils, with clay (CL, CH) as the main type of soil in the mixtures. The incomplete
data-set was derived by i) removing records from the TUM Geo database, where some
properties have values with no physical meaning (e.g negative cohesion or friction angle), and
ii) removing records containing propertiesвҖҷ measurements with extreme values or not logical
values for the considered type of soils, based on some experience from past investigations in
the region. More specifically, for ii) the initial processing included the following:

4.1.3 TUM-TRX_CLAY_22 Regional Database
The constructed incomplete data-set of fine-grained, cohesive soils, was used as a basis to
derive the TUM-TRX_CLAY_22 database, which was used in the further Analysis. It is
generally known, that data outliers affect any statistical analysis, even more a multivariate
statistical analysis. Outlier detection often requires strong knowledge on the physics and
mechanics of the soil properties and parameters, and is not a trivial task, especially for
75
==PAGE:81==

Ei,ПғвҖІ
3, E50,ПғвҖІ

3 (MPa)
confining stress dependent YoungвҖҷs moduli, from stress-strain curves; CU triaxial tests
Table 4.4: Description of attributes in the Fine-grained, cohesive soilsвҖҷ dataset
spatially variable parameters, collected from a large number of sites. Machine learning
methods, such as unsupervised clustering (e.g. Herlau et al., 2020), can be adopted in such
cases. However the implementation of such methods is not trivial, if one considers i) the
incomplete feature of the geotechnical data, ii) the soil natural and spatial variability.
Having in mind these data characteristics, the procedure for identifying bad quality
data, was mainly based on physical bounds of the soil parameters, i.e. data that are not
representative of reasonable soil parametersвҖҷ values in the considered soil types. In addition,
a simplified version of a probability density based approach was adopted to remove some
possible outliers. The method known as Kernel density estimation, can be used to compute a
smooth estimate of the PDF or jpdf of random variables. For details of the method the reader
is referred to (e.g Herlau et al., 2020). In this study, a simplified version of the method was
adopted, and this involved removal of measurements that fall in regions of very low bivariate
joint probability density. In general, the KDE method can be extended to multivariate (n>2
dimensions) data.
For instance, a simple function was programmed to remove a percentage of the total number
of cohesion and friction angle measurements. Since these parameters are often estimated
with a simple linear regression model (M-C criterion), the derived values can be prompt to
large errors. To identify anomalies in the sets of observations,
вҖў A density threshold of 3% was assumed
вҖў NaNs were removed from the bivariate dataset
вҖў The joint probability density was calculated from the complete set of data, using the
function "gaussian_kde" from the Scipy library www-03
in Python
76
==PAGE:82==
4 Database overview and Elementary data Analysis
вҖў Values that fell in probability density regions less than the desired threshold were
replaced with NaNs.
It is important to note that the estimation of the joint PDF with KDE can have low accuracy,
and it can be misleading in some cases. Hence, the kde method should be used with caution
and should include logical reasoning for the soil propertiesвҖҷ physical bounds. Moreover,
the multivariate generic database, should include reasonably high uncertainty in the soil
properties, since the data were collected from many sites and different conditions. Hence, the
kde based outliersвҖҷ removal, was only applied in the initial stage of bad data detection, with
a low density threshold.
The next steps in the data cleansing stage, were based on the following:
вҖў Nan replacement to measurements that are higher or lower than 1.5 Г— iqr (%), where
iqr the interquartile range. See Appendix Table A.1 for quartiles, 25 (%) and 75 (%)
вҖў Records where fines (uscs) <60% were removed, since it was assumed that the sand
content can impact the propertiesвҖҷ behavior
вҖў Records at depths less than 1.5m were removed (possible different behavior in shallow
depths)
вҖў Records where su,uc > 700 kPa where removed, based on visual inspection and on
published representative bounds of the parameter in clayey soils (e.g. see Table 3.1)
Descriptive statistics based on the updated incomplete data-set are given in Table 4.5. The
derived data-set, named in this project as TUM-TRX_CLAY_22 serves as the basis for further
analysis. Thus, it was considered that the database retains information from many different
sites, and a reasonably high amount of uncertainty in the soil properties, while containing
measurements on soil properties, in comparable soil conditions. Hence it can be representative
of the soil properties, strength and elasticity parametersвҖҷ, in the region. The database contains:
вҖў 1220 (incomplete) records
вҖў data from an approximate number of 400 unique projects
вҖў 1095 classified soils, with soil classes shown in Figure 4.4.
Elementary plots (boxplots) are provided in Figure 4.4 for selected properties and parame-
ters, based on the TUM-TRX_CLAY_22 data and the descriptive statistics given in Table 4.5.
Pairwise information and correlation plots
The number of pairwise data-points between the numerical attributes in the dataset is given in
Table 4.6. Note that since the pairwise count is symmetric, only the upper diagonal is shown
to avoid confusion. The diagonal of the matrix is equal to the univariate number of data-
points given in Table 4.5. It can be seen in Table 4.6, that even though the data-set contains a
77
==PAGE:83==
4 Database overview and Elementary data Analysis

large number of pairwise data-points between index properties and sieve analysis results,
it does not contain many pairwise data-points between these test indices, and the strength
related parameters. Moreover, it can be noticed that the number of datapoints between the
strength parameters from TC tests, and the undrained shear strength from UC tests, is very
low, to obtain meaningful information on their statistical dependence (correlation). Hence,
it was decided to split the data-set into two subsets: one containing information between
sieve-plasticity and the TC testsвҖҷ parameters, and the second one containing data from sieve-
plasticity and undrained shear strength from the UC tests. In addition the attributes that
give a reasonable amount of pairwise information was kept (>35 data-pairs). The selected
attributes and the number of pairwise data-points for these two scenarios (subsets), are
provided in Table 4.7 and Table 4.8, respectively. Note that the first 15 rows and columns have
the same elements in these Tables, since they correspond to the same soil properties. Based
on the number of pairwise data-points it can be said that:
вҖў The number of pairwise data-points between the TC derived parameters, and the rest
soil properties in Table 4.7, may be not large enough to obtain reliable estimations
of their dependency, from the computed sample correlation coefficients. However, it
includes pairwise information on more than one design parameters.
вҖў A larger number of pairwise data-points between the undrained shear strength (UC),
and the rest soil properties in Table 4.7 can be observed. This number may be sufficient
enough to get a more reliable point estimate of the correlation between the soil properties
and the design parameter su,uc.
The Pearson moment correlation coefficients were computed from the pairwise information.
Figures 4.6-4.5 illustrate the magnitude of the pairwise correlation coefficients for each of the
two subsets. Note that the first 15 rows and columns have the same elements in these Tables.
Figures A1-A2 in the Appendix, visualize the pairwise correlation structures between the
considered soil parameters in each sub-dataset (note: log-transformed variables plotted in
these figures).
78
==PAGE:84==
4 Database overview and Elementary data Analysis
In Figures 4.6,4.5, the scaling between red and blue colors indicate the magnitude of the
correlation coefficients. Red colored cells indicate positive correlation between the parameters,
while blue colored cells indicate negative correlation. Specific values of the sample correlation
coefficients are given in Tables 4.9, 4.10. It is reasonable that very high absolute correlation
coefficient values, belong to properties that are naturally related to each other, such as the
dry density ПҒd and water content w, or the initial void ratio e0.
Based on (Evans, 1996) the correlation coefficients can be classified as shown in Table 4.11.
It is evident that most parameters in the database are "weakly" or "very weakly" correlated.
However, there exist some interesting correlations between some parameters, such as,
вҖў Moderate positive: Undrained shear strength su,ucand dry density ПҒd, youngвҖҷs modulus
E50,200 and consistency index ic
вҖў Moderate negative: Undrained shear strength su,uc and dry density ПҒd, youngвҖҷs modulus
E50,200 and liquidity index LI, friction angle П• and plasticity index PI, ratio of max shear
and average stresses (q/p)200 and clay content %clay
Of course some of the positive and negative moderate correlations can be explained by the
direct (negative) relationship between properties such as the water content, dry density and
void ratio. However, the sample statistics reveal the existence of considerable inter-correlations
between parameters in the database, which means that a multivariate analysis is justified
and recommended. Statistical uncertainties can be substantially reduced even when the soil
parameters are weakly correlated (e.g. c вҲ’П•). On the other hand, very weak correlations, can
provide guidance on which parameters can be neglected for the Multivariate Analysis.

==PAGE:92==
5 Results
This chapter focuses on the implementation of described methods in the previous chapter.
It starts with the application of Bayesian inference on selected parameters included in the
regional database. Bayesian parameter estimation based on the regional data, provides rec-
ommendations on the selection of marginal distributions for several soil parameters in the
regional database. Univariate posterior and predictive distributions from the observed re-
gional data, can be utilized as prior distributions in the updating process. These distributions
can then be conditioned on observed site investigation data, to derive site-specific updated
marginal distributions of the soil properties.
The Gaussian copula framework, and the Nataf model is applied to construct joint dis-
tributions of the soil parameters in the regional database. The best-fit univariate marginals
were used in this stage. The constructed distributions can be used as multivariate prior
distributions, and can then be conditioned on site-investigation data, through the Bayesian
updating stages.
In the subsequent stage, a multivariate distribution based on the JohnsonвҖҷs system of
distributions, was constructed based on the regional data. The regional model serves as a
prior model, containing the prior knowledge about the soil parameters in the region. The
performance of the hybridization, and of the conventional Bayesian updating methods, is
investigated based on a specific local site, in a real case study.
5.1 Constructing a regional multivariate model with different
marginal distributions
5.1.1 Identifying the best-fit marginals for the shear strength parameters
The four candidate models in Table 5.1 were considered for modeling the Mohr-Coulomb
shear strength parameters. For the parameters a, b of the Beta model, constant values were

87
==PAGE:93==
5 Results
Analytical expressions of the probabilistic models and the distribution parameters are not
provided here. The reader is referred to the Appendix of the (ERA Group, 2020) document
for the latter three models, while for the first model, analytical expressions are provided in
(K. K. Phoon and Ching, 2015, pp. 94). The MLE and Bayesian methods were implemented
to perform the following steps:
1. compute the parameters of 4 candidate probabilistic models and evaluate their posterior
joint distribution
2. compute the exact predictive distributions with numerical integration
3. select the model that best describes the laboratory data
A Python program was constructed to perform the methods described in detail in sections
3.1.2, 3.1.3, 3.1.4, 3.2. Analytical expressions of the likelihood functions (and log-likelihood
functions) were used and these can be found in the python scripts provided in the supple-
mentary material of this document. The computation steps can be briefly summarized in the
following:
вҖў Uniform distributions as priors (see Equation 3.11. Prior bounds were assigned to each
strength parameter based on published literature (e.g. see Table 3.1). These bounds were
then used to define the joint uniform prior of the distribution parameters. A random
sample was drawn from the joint uniform prior for the mean and standard deviation of
the random variables, and the parameters of each PDF were computed based on these
values.
вҖў For each candidate model the MLE point estimates of the distribution parameters
were computed, along with the estimation error of the method (see section 3.1.2). The
likelihood function value was then computed at the ML estimates.
вҖў The proportionality constant was computed with numerical integration over the pa-
rametersвҖҷ space and the posterior distribution was evaluated for each model. Point
estimates of the distribution parameters were obtained from the posterior mean (see
Equation 3.7).
вҖў The predictive CDF of each candidate model was evaluated based on Equation 3.13.
вҖў For selecting the best model among the candidates:
- The AIC and BIC measures (see Equations 3.14, 3.15) were calculated using the
value of the likelihood function at the ML estimates.
- Bayesian model class selection was performed. Equal prior probability was as-
signed to each candidate model, the posterior model probability was computed
based Equation 3.16.
вҖў Model fusion was performed based on the predictive CDFs of the candidate models,
utilizing the posterior model probabilities Pr(Mi|Z) as weights.
88
==PAGE:94==
5 Results
The results from every aforementioned step was stored in nested Python dictionaries.
Results for the M-C strength parameters are provided in the following subsections for the
following subsets of the regional database (see Table 4.5):
1. Entire set of c and П• measurements in the regional database TUM-TRX_CL_22 (see
Table 4.5, except 4 samples labeled as silts (ML, MH)
2. Labeled measurements with number of observations n > 40:
a) Consolidated Undrained (CU)
b) Clays of low plasticity (CL)
The results of the MLE approach along with the estimation error, and the results of the
Bayesian Analysis for each of the four candidate models are provided in Table 5.2. Note that
for the model M4 (Beta distr.) the associated mean and standard deviation of the random
variable is given. These were calculated from the estimated parameters of the Beta distribution
(see ERA Group, 2020). In addition, no results are provided for the normal model M1 in
the analysis of the effective cohesion c, since (after testing) it was judged as inappropriate to
describe the observations.
The results of the Bayesian parameter estimation and the posterior probability of each
candidate model are based on the following prior bounds:
вҖў prior bounds for cohesion c:
- For mean: Вө вҲҲ[1, 60] (kPa)
- For standard deviation: Пғ вҲҲ[8, 18] (kPa)
вҖў prior bounds for friction angle П•
- For mean: Вө вҲҲ[17, 40] (o)
- For standard deviation: Пғ вҲҲ[3, 8] (o)
For the Beta model, the parameters [a, b] were predefined as:
вҖў For cohesion c: [0, 200] (kPa)
вҖў For friction angle П•: [0, 45] (o)
The parameters [a, b] were defined to represent physical limits of the cohesion and friction
angle in the considered conditions, based on published literature and the descriptive statistics
of the regional database (see Tables 3.1, 4.5). From Table 5.2 the information criteria AIC, BIC
(minimum) and the bayesian posterior probability Pr(Mi|Z) (maximum) suggest the models
given in Table 5.3.
Figure 5.1 illustrates a 2d plot of the posterior jpdf of the Weibull distribution parameters.
The Weibull distribution was identified as the best-fit distribution for the friction angle,
based on the entire TUM_TRX_CL_22 dataset, and the dataset containing the labeled CL
measurements. The colorbar in Figure 5.1, indicates the joint probability density in the

numerical integration. The fused predictive distribution was evaluated as a mixture of
the considered models, based on each modelвҖҷs posterior probability Pr(Mi|Z). These are
demonstrated in Figure 5.2, corresponding to the entire regional dataset, and in Figure 5.3,
based on the CL labeled measurements. Similar results with the regional distributions were
obtained using the CU dataset, hence these are not illustrated here.
In both Figures 5.2, 5.3, can be noticed that the fused distribution follows closely the
identified best-fit marginals. Differences are becoming evident in the case of the CL dataset,
Figure 5.3, although considering the PDF and CDF of the effective cohesion, these are very
close to the Beta distribution, due to the high posterior probability of the model (see Table
5.2). This can be explained by the choice of uniform priors, and the relatively large number
of observations in the regional dataset. The likelihood strongly affects the posterior in this
case. The benefits of applying the Bayesian method become more clear when a lower number
of observations are available, although according to reliability standards, a number >30 of
observations are usually needed for more reliable predictions. Within the context of a local
site investigation that usually provides only a basket of observations, the proposed Bayesian
method can provide a reference basis for the uncertainty at the site.

5.1.2 The multivariate regional distribution based on the Nataf model
The Nataf model was adopted to construct an 8-parameter multivariate distribution, based
on the following considerations:
вҖў Best-fit distributions for M-C shear strength parameters, obtained with Bayesian analysis
вҖў Selection of distributions for rest soil properties based on visual inspection and literature
review, and Maximum Likelihood estimation for their distribution parameters.
вҖў Input correlation matrix R constructed from Pearson sample correlation coefficients (see
Table 4.9.
The considered variables (X1, X2, ..., X8) along with the estimated distribution parameters
can be seen in Table 5.4, and the correlation matrix in Table 5.5. For the definition of the
parameters, the reader is referred to (ERA Group, 2020).

and the joint normality assumption. The steps for simulating dependent samples from the
joint distribution were discussed in detail in sections 3.3.4, 3.3.5. The ERANataf ERA Group,

correlation matrix R0 (see Equation 3.36), is given in Table 5.6. A large number (n = 2 В· 105) of
dependent samples were simulated from the joint Nataf distribution. The simulation results
are demonstrated in the form of bivariate correlations in Figure 5.4 for correlations between
friction angle П•, Figure 5.5 for cohesion c, and Figure 5.6 for the elasticity modulus E50,200.
The 95% Confidence Intervals were constructed from the total number of simulated samples
(2000 of them are plotted).
Figure 5.4 indicates some interesting correlation structures between friction angle and
plasticity index PI, in which the CIs are relatively narrow. Such correlation has reported
in the literature as well (e.g. see F. Kulhawy and P. Mayne, 1990). Very narrow CIs can
be noticed between (П•, (q/p)200, which are physically strong correlated. However, such a
correlation can be useful for estimating with confidence the friction angle from one confining
stress level in a triaxial test. With regard to to the correlation (c, П•), the large uncertainty in
the cohesion parameter is evident, although a weak negative correlation between the M-C
parameters is reported. The plotted samples and CIs in Figure indicate a weak negative
correlation between (LI, c). Observing the correlation structures in Figure 5.6, a moderate
negative correlation was captured between (LI, E50,200). Negative liquidity index values can
indicate over-consolidated soils (e.g. see Das, 2015), and a correlation within these liquidity
index range could potentially be explained by this relation.
The marginal CDFs evaluated using the simulated samples from the Nataf distribution,
are demonstrated in Figures 5.7. Figure 5.8 illustrates the constructed marginal CDFs, and
the empirical CDF computed from limited sparse observations of a local site "U5-Freiham"
(see Table 5.7), which is considered as test dataset in in this study. The provided marginal
distributions can be used as prior distributions, and these can be updated based on the site
data to allow more reliable site predictions (see Figure 2.6). In some cases, such as the regional
CDF of friction angle, or the regional CDF of E50,200, the constructed distributions appear to
describe reasonably well the limited site observations.
The simulation results of the constructed Nataf distribution, are directly comparable to
those presented later in this chapter, in section 5.2.1. The differences between the two models
lies on the selection of the marginal distributions, the method for estimating their parameters,
and the calculation of the correlation matrix.

5.2 Constructing a site-specific Multivariate probability distribution
for triaxial testing parameters
The construction of a site specific multivariate probability distribution for selected parameters
from the triaxial dataset, is presented in this section. The case study is based on the site
investigation data given in Table 5.7 for the clayey soil layers at the site U5-Freiham in Munich,
Germany. 11 soil parameters were considered in this scenario, as can be seen in Table 5.7.

Table 5.7: Site investigation data: Freiham U5, Munich Germany; Data collected from the

The selection of the parameters was based on i) the number of observations for each
parameter in the regional database (see Table 4.5), ii) the number of pairwise information
(see Table 4.7) and iii) the indication of possible correlations from the sample correlation
coefficients (see Figure 4.6 and Table 4.9), and their classification given in Table 4.11. It
is evident that the site-investigation data are sparse, with many missing entries matrix.
Furthermore, the measurements on some properties such as d50 and %clay are completely
missing. Due to the data sparsity, all data-points are used as training site data in this study.
Note however, that the site-specific data were removed from the regional database before the
analysis ("leave one out", e.g. see (Herlau et al., 2020; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019)).
5.2.1 The regional multivariate distribution based on JohnsonвҖҷs marginals
Distribution fitting
The JohnsonвҖҷs marginal distribution for each of the 11 soil parameters is defined in the first
step, using the full regional data. The logarithm transform is applied to every property that
101
==PAGE:107==
5 Results
can not take negative values. Hence, the following 11 variables (Y1, Y2, ..., Y11) are defined,
Y1 = ln(d50),
Y2 = ln(%clay),
Y3 = ln(w),
Y4 = ln(LL),
Y5 = ln(PI),
Y6 = ic,
Y7 = ln(ПҒd),
Y8 = ln((q/p)200),
Y9 = ln(c),
Y10 = ln(П•),
Y11 = ln(E50,200)
(5.1)
The JohnsonвҖҷs marginal distribution type for each of the 11 variables (Y1, Y2, ..., Y11), and
the parameters of each distribution were computed following the steps described in section
3.4.2. The fitted JohnsonвҖҷs distributions (red continuous lines) are visualized in Figure 5.9
along with the empirical distributions (grey circles) derived from the generic soil data. A
visual inspection indicates almost exact fit of the JohnsonвҖҷs distribution to the empirical
distributions for the first 7 variables. These correspond to the variables with the larger
number of observations. It can be also said, that the distribution-fitting results are satisfying,
even for the last 4 variables that correspond to the shear strength and elasticity parameters,
and for which the number of data-points is considerably lower. It can be noticed that 6
variables can be modeled with the SU (unbounded) JohnsonвҖҷs member, while the strength
parameters, except from the q/p ratio, and the elasticity modulus were identified as SB
(bounded between [by, ay + by]) members. No SL members were identified, whose relation to
the shifted lognormal distribution is reminded.
The translation Y вҶ’X, i.e. transformation of the non-normal random variables to the to
the standard normal space, was performed following the closed form expressions given in
Equation 3.61. Subsequently, the Kolmogorov-Smirnov (K-S) test, discussed in section 3.4.3,
was performed to check the normality of the transformed variables. The distribution-fitting
results are presented in table 5.8. The third column indicates the identified JohnsonвҖҷs member
for each random variable. In columns 4-7, the computed distributional parameters are given.
The last column of the Table, corresponds to the p-value associated with the K-S test, and
provides indication on whether to accept or reject the marginal normality hypothesis of the
transformed variables Xi. For the chosen a = 0.05 significance level, the p-values indicate that
the null hypothesis is not rejected for all Xi, meaning that the transformed variables can be
considered (marginally) as standard normal variables.
In accordance with the works of (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching, Lin, et al., 2017;
Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), the multivariate nor-
mality assumption is adopted in the present study, as well. This means that the transformed
variables (X1, X2, ..., X11), are assumed to jointly follow the multi-Normal distribution (see
also section 3.3.4). Hence, having computed the marginal distributions, the next step was to
compute a valid correlation matrix, to completely define the multivariate distribution model.
The construction of the correlation matrix is presented in the next section.

Bootstrapping correlation coefficients
In chapter 3 and section 3.4.4, the concept of pairwise correlations between two soil parameters
(Yi, Yj) was introduced. It is reminded, that the correlation concept can be related to whether
measuring a soil property Yi, can lead to reduction in the uncertainty of another soil property
Yj. Recall the classification of the sample correlation coefficients (according to (Evans, 1996)),
which were computed from the data, and they were presented in Table 4.11. The correlation
classification revealed that some of the considered soil parameters may present a considerable
degree of linear correlation. For instance, in Table 4.11 there is an indication of "moderate"
correlation between friction angle П• and Plasticity index PI. This means that the measurement
of plasticity index (or Y5) can cause a reduction in the variance of the friction angle (Y9).
For moderate correlations the variance reduction percentage is approximately 25% (1 вҲ’ПҒ2
ij)
(Ching, Lin, et al., 2017). On the other hand, it was pointed out that the number of pairwise
data-points is not sufficient enough to get unbiased estimations on the correlation between
these parameters, directly from the sample coefficients (see Table 4.7). It is crucial, as well,
that a correlation matrix constructed directly from the respective correlation coefficients in
Table 4.7), is not a positive-definite matrix.
To address, to some degree, the statistical uncertainty that arise from the relatively poor
data coverage in the pairwise data-points of some soil parameters, and obtain a valid positive
definite correlation matrix, the rejection-sampling version of the bootstrap technique was
implemented following section 3.4.4. A large number, n = 10000, of samples, representative
of the Pearson (product moment) correlation coefficient pairs of (Xi, Xj), was generated. The
large number of samples was assumed sufficient enough, to provide a more reliable estimation
of the true statistic value, as an average of the total accepted generated samples ПҒij. In Table
the resulting correlation coefficients that lead to a valid positive definite correlation matrix,
is presented. The bootstrap correlation coefficients can be compared to the to the sample
Pearson correlation coefficients computed from the original soil data, given in Table 4.9. A
closer look at the two tables can generally reveal a reduction in the correlation coefficients,

mainly in those that refer to the triaxial testing parameters and some selected soil properties
