Simulation from the regional unconditional multivariate distribution
The (standard) multi-normal distribution of (X1, X2, ..., X11) was constructed based on the
identified JohnsonвҖҷs marginals, and the resulting correlation matrix containing the accepted
coefficients of Table 5.9. This distribution is named here as unconditional regional JPDF. The
simulation of dependent soil samples from the joint distribution was discussed in detail
in section 3.4.4. The results on the correlations between the friction angle П• and the rest
soil parameters in the dataset are provided in Figure 5.10 Simulated samples from the
unconditional JPDF are plotted (grey circles) along with the pairwise data-points in the
regional database, and with the computed moving mean, and Confidence Intervals. The
CIвҖҷs correspond to the (2.5%, 97.5%) percentiles, i.e. 95% CIs. The illustrated 95% CIs were
computed from a large number n = 2 В· 106 samples. 2000 of them are shown in each subplot
of Figure 5.10. A visual inspection reveals that:
вҖў The site data (red circles) are within the coverage of the regional database (black circles),
and the data can be assumed to follow same mean and covariance matrix. Under this
homogeneity assumption both Bayesian and Hybrid methods are applicable.
вҖў The CIs become narrower in regions were a larger number of simulated samples fall
вҖў The statistical uncertainty is quite high, in the regional data. This can be noticed in the
CIs of most bivariate correlations.
вҖў Some possibly interesting correlation structures can be noticed between friction angle
and the index properties. Moreover, a negative correlation can be observed between
cohesion and friction angle, which is larger than the regional soil data showed.
104
==PAGE:110==
5 Results
вҖў П• вҲ’E50,200 appear to be uncorrelated, since the simulated data are uniformly scattered
in the E50 range. Very weak correlation appears to be present between П• вҲ’ПҒd in the
regional data, although the site data show a positive trend which could be a sign for
higher correlation degree at the site, between the two parameters.
вҖў A strong correlation exists between П• вҲ’(q/p)200, which was expected to some degree,
if one considers the way these parameters are computed. The two parameters presented
also very strong correlation, based on the regional data.
вҖў The correlation structures justify the choice of parameters for the construction of a
site-specific multivariate model. The uncertainty of the friction angle, can be reduced in
the updating process, due to the existing correlations between the selected parameters.
Correlation structures between c, E50,200, (q/p)200 and the rest soil properties, as obtained
from simulation from the unconditional regional PDF, can be viewed in the Appendix (Figures
A.3, A.7, A.11).

5.2.2 The quasi-Site-specific model based on Bayesian updating with conjugate
priors and Gibbs Sampling
A brief discussion was made on the simulation results of the unconditional regional multivari-
ate distribution. It is reminded that the "conventional" updating of the regional distribution
based on a set of measurements on soil parameters at the site, was discussed in section 3.5.1
(see Example 2). The results that are presented in this section, are related to the Bayesian
updating process using conjugate priors and the Gibbs sampler to obtain posterior samples of
the parameters (mean vectors and covariance matrix), following sections 3.5.2-3.5.4.
The Bayesian updating of the regional predictive distribution f (x|Xdb,o), based on the site
data given in Table 5.7, (see section 3.5.4, pp. 66) was performed, and the PDF was then
conditioned on the site investigation data, to construct the distribution f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o). The
hybridization method between the regional PDF, and the Site-specific predictive PDF f (x|Xs,o)
constructed from the limited sparse data of table 5.7, was tested as well. For the calculation
of stable statistics, such as median confidence intervals and correlations, the GS sampler
was programmed with total sampling step size equal to T = 20000, and a burn-in period
of tb = 1000. Figure demonstrates i) a histogram of collected correlation coefficient pairs ПҒ,
between friction angle and the rest parameters, from the posterior samples obtained with
Gibbs Sampling on the regional dataset, ii) the bootsrap average of accepted drawn correlation
coefficients as a vertical red line, and iii) the sample Pearson correlation coefficients computed
from the transformed to the normal space regional data as a vertical black line. Some
comments on Figure 5.11:
вҖў The bootstrap technique produces the lowest correlation coefficients in most cases,
except ПҒ9,10 (cohesion-friction angle) where the results are very close to the sample
coefficient from the regional data, and to the peak of the GS samplesвҖҷ histogram. This
could be an indication of a "weakness" of the bootstrap technique to produce correlation
coefficients of high magnitude.
вҖў The GS samplesвҖҷ histograms indicate higher correlation in most parametersвҖҷ pairs. The
histogram of ПҒ3,10 and ПҒ6,10 illustrates an unsure correlation between these parameters
and friction angle, since the bars are extended from negative to positive values. It
should be noted that (X1, X11) are roughly standard normal, meaning that standard
deviations of the properties are not exactly one (and means are not exactly zero). For
very strong correlations, i.e. ПҒ8,10 the GS posterior histogram indicates some values
larger than 1, which do not represent actual correlation coefficient values ([-1,1]). on the
pother hand, the GS histograms suggest that higher correlation can exist between most
soil parameters in the regional data.
The regional predictive PDF was first evaluated based on the regional triaxial related dataset,
and this distribution was then conditioned on the observed site investigation data (Table 5.7)
to produce the "quasi-site-specific" model f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o), as a mixture of multivariate normal
PDFs. Random samples X were drawn from the distribution, and these were converted
physical samples Y following the inverse transform, and subsequently to soil samples, to
108
==PAGE:114==
5 Results
investigate possible correlation structures between the soil parameters. Similarly, soil samples
were obtained from the hybrid distribution f (x|hb). The simulation results on the soil
parameters cross-correlations with the design parameter friction angle, and the comparison
between the Bayesian and Hybrid methods are demonstrated in Figure 5.12 and Figure 5.13,
respectively. These figures provide a comparison between the Bayesian and Hybridization
methods, with the simulation results from the unconditional regional PDF and from the
Site-specific predictive PDF constructed from the limited site data of table 5.7. The methodsвҖҷ
comparison on the correlation structure between friction angle and the rest soil parameters
in the TC dataset, is illustrated in Figure 5.14 .Note that the Confidence intervals (dashed
blue lines) and the median (red line) in Figure 5.14, were computed based on the entire set of
simulated samples from the updated regional predictive PDF, i.e. f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o).
Comments on the correlations between friction angle and the rest soil parameters, in Figures
5.12-5.14:
вҖў Both tested methods, Bayesian and Hybridization, showed reduced uncertainty, in
comparison with the simulation results from the unconditional regional PDF (see Figure
5.10. This can be noticed in the reduced scatter of the plotted simulated samples in
Figures 5.12, 5.13.
вҖў The uncertainty at the site is high due to sparse and limited observations. This is
reflected by the Site-Specific predictive PDFs (blue dots), and the large scatter in the
simulated samples from this distribution. Note that when no available pairwise obser-
vations exist at the site (first two plots), the simulated samples from this distribution
are scattered over the region defined by the soil parameterвҖҷs bounds.
вҖў The Bayesian methodвҖҷs results in Figure 5.12, indicated higher correlation between
friction angle and the rest the soil parameters. The higher degree of negative correla-
tions between (LL, П•), (PI, П•) and (ic, П•), in comparison to the respective ones shown
in Figure 5.10, is evident. Even though the updating of the regional f (x|Xdb,o) to
f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o), was performed based only on 1 pairwise observationsвҖҷ set (1 site data-
point on the plot), the simulation results provide a better picture on their "site-specific"
dependence. (However, the utilization of the predictive regional f (x|Xdb,o) plays an
important role on this, hence the simulated X samples approach this distribution when
no, or very low number of observations are available at the site).
вҖў Negative correlation exists at the site between (c, П•). Reduction in the uncertainty
is evident from the reduction in the scatter of simulated samples, and more reliable
predictions can be made at the site level.
вҖў The illustrated CIs in Figure 5.14 were computed from Bayesian updating of the regional
predictive PDF. The CIs confirm the uncertainty reduction in comparison with the
simulation results from the unconditional distribution presented in Figure 5.10. This
uncertainty reduction can arise from:
- The updating of the regional PDF based on the observed regional data and the
simulation from the predictive regional PDF. In addition, the conditioning of the
109
==PAGE:115==
5 Results
regional predictive PDF based on the observed site data, reduced further the
uncertainty in those correlations, where a larger number of pairwise data-points
was available. This can be noticed in the mainly in the correlations between (q/p, П•)
(very strong) and (П•, ПҒd). For the latter, a positive correlation appears apply at the
site.
вҖў The last comment refers to the comparison between the Bayesian and Hybridization
methodsвҖҷ results. It is important to note that in the implementation of the hybrid method,
it was considered that f (x|Xdb,o) = f (x|Вөg, Cg), or in other words, the unconditional
regional PDF (Figure 5.10), with covariance matrix obtained from the bootstrap samples,
was utilized in the position of the actual regional predictive PDF f (x|Xdb,o), to produce
the hybrid PDF as f (x|hb) = f (x|0, Cdb) Г— f (x|Xs,o) (see section 3.5.6 or (Ching and
K. K. Phoon, 2019)). This consideration is believed that it affects the methodsвҖҷ results,
since the pairwise observations between the triaxial testing derived parameters, and the
rest soil properties (e.g. index properties), in both regional and site specific datasets
(TC dataset Table 4.7), are probably not sufficiently large in numbers to justify this
assumption. The method performs under the framework which states that: "when the
site data are too sparse (or completely missing), the hybrid PDF will approach the
regional PDF" (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019). Moreover, it was shown that a limitation
of the bootstrap technique, is that it provides lower correlation coefficients that may
apply in reality.
This can be also noticed in the correlations of Figure 5.10, where the are not available site
data-points (e.g. correlations with d50, %clay). In these cases, the simulation results from
the hybrid distribution are essentially very similar to the ones from the unconditional
distribution, presented in Figure 5.10. For correlations where only one observationsвҖҷ set
was available (i.e with LL, PI, w and ic) the scatter of simulated samples is reduced
in comparison with the plotted unconditional samples in Figure 5.10. On the other
hand, the scatter of the hybrid simulated samples, with regard to these cases, shows
reduced covariance between these parameters, and a lower degree of correlation with
friction angle. The simulated samples are more concentrated around a specific region,
representative to the pairwise observations at the site. The fact that only one pairwise
data-point was available at the site, was assumed that it cannot lead to reliable CIs
with the hybrid method for these correlations. However, it is evident that even one
paiwise data-point leads to considerable reduction in the scatter, in comparison to the
unconditional samples. The hybrid method appears to be able to capture the correlation
structure when more pairwise site observations are available, i.e. П• with (q/p)200, ПҒd.
With regards to (ПҒd, П•), the hybrid method reduces the scatter and produced slightly
narrower CIs that the Bayesian approach. The Hybrid method can be also repeated
by utilizing f (x|Xdb,o) instead of the unconditional PDF. Moreover, if more data (and
pairwise observations) become available, a better comparison between the Bayesian and
Hybridization can be made.
Bivariate correlation structures between the index properties and the triaxial parameters
110
==PAGE:116==
5 Results
(q/p)200, c, E50,200, are provided in the Appendix (Figures A.4, A.5, A.6, A.8, A.9, A.10, A.12,
A.13).
For E50,200 the simulated samples from the unconditional regional PDF indicated large
uncertainty. High uncertainty was evident in the visualized bivariate correlations, constructed
from the simulated samples of the conditional predictive PDF with the Bayesian approach,
even though the scatter was reduced. It turned out that the hybridization method led to
reduced uncertainty and slightly narrower CIs in the correlations of E50,200. In any case,
it is believed that more pairwise observations are needed to reveal more clear correlation
structures, although the results provided in the Appendix (Figures A.12, A.13) showed that
there may exist some interesting correlations between E50,200, moisture content and index
properties. The site-specific results can be used as a reference for site-specific predictions, and
validation can be performed if more observations become available at the site. Note that all
Site-Specific CIs were computed based on samples from f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o).
Interesting results were obtained for the effective cohesion, as well. It is generally known
that it is very difficult to reliably measure the effective cohesion in laboratory tests (e.g. LADE,
2016), especially in clayey soils, and even for overconsolidated clays the true cohesion can be
approximately zero. This is a limitation of the Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion, which may
describe well in many cases the data, but it is not expected to provide reliable results in every
case. According to (LADE, 2016, pp. 7) only cemented soils appear to have true cohesion of
any significant magnitude. Hence this parameter is expected to include high uncertainty.
Figure 5.14 illustrates an approximation of the bivariate PDF for some selected parameters.
Note that there exist no analytical expression for the bivariate PDFs. Hence, to obtain a
smooth estimate of the bivariate distribution, n = 15000 pairwise simulated samples were
used, and the Kernel Density estimation (Herlau et al., 2020; www-03 ) functions were used to
derive the bivariate PDF approximation. Note the PDF estimates with the KDE, are highly
dependent on the selection of the bandwidths (among others), and Gaussian kernels are
used. Hence, the numerical values of the PDFs should be used with caution, and those
referring to the shear strength parameters, may have low accuracy and be misleading. Here
a set of candidate values for the bandwidths was pre-defined, and "Leave-one-out" cross
validation (Herlau et al., 2020) was used to compute the optimal bandwidth. The results
were very close to those of the "silverman" method (see scipy.stats.gaussian_kde in www-03
). The distribution shapes can be useful for identifying the dependence structure between
the selected soil parameters, and these can be compared to Figures 5.12, 5.13, where the
cloud of simulated samples represents their joint distribution. The colorbars in each subplot
represent the probability density of the colored regions in the graphs, and the iso-contour
lines correspond to regions with the same probability density.
It can be noticed in Figure 5.14i, that the evaluated with KDE PDF, assigns some density
in the region of negative cohesion values. This is not possible in reality, however it can be
explained by the many low cohesion values in the regional TC dataset (e.g. see statistics in
4.5, Figure 5.10), and the KDE approximation of the PDF with Gaussian kernels. Hence it
is recommended not to use this (c, П•) PDF approximation for reliability purposes, and one
should utilize the simulated samples in this case (e.g. computation of failure probabilities). In
111
==PAGE:117==
5 Results
addition, it can be observed in the same Figure 5.14i, and in Figure 5.14j, as well, that many
site observations fall in regions of very low (kernel) density. This means that the uncertainty
is high at the site for the cohesion parameter, and due to low pairwise observations at the site,
in relevance to the regional dataset, both Hybrid and Bayesian (in higher degree) methods
are mainly affected by the regional data.
In all other subplots of Figure 5.14, the PDFs with KDE, provide a good picture of the
correlation structure between friction angle and the rest soil properties, and can represent
regions in which new observations will fall with confidence. Similar remarks with those of
Figure 5.14 can be made: The Bayesian based computed PDFs appear to be more slanted,
indicating higher covariance (and correlation) between the soil parametersвҖҷ pairs, while the
PDFs values are lower than those of the Hybrid method, since the simulated samples are
distributed over a wider region (soil parameter in the x-axis), which was observed in Figure
5.14, as well. This can be explained again by the low amount of pairwise observations at
the site in relevance to those of the regional dataset, hence the Bayesian based simulation
appears to carry more information about the uncertainty in the soil parameters at a regional
scale, than the respective simulation results from the hybridization method. The obtained
simulation results on the cross-correlations between plasticity index PI and the rest soil
properties are demonstrated in Figure 5.15. The subplot indicating the correlation between
LL вҲ’PI is probably the one with the most interest. Based on the Plasticity chart (Figure 4.3),
the simulation results from the Hybrid PDF, indicate "medium" to "high" plastic clays at the
site, and a narrow CI. A positive correlation between the water content and the plasticity index
is demonstrated in the 3rd subplot, which was not captured in that scale by the simulation
from conditional predictive f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o).

5.2.3 Marginal distributions
The simulated samples can be used to construct the updated marginal CDFs of the variables.
The simulated samples X from the multivariate distributions:
вҖў Site specific predictive: f (x|Xs,o)
вҖў Conditional predictive f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o)
вҖў Hybrid f (x|hb)
were translated to Y samples following the inverse transform, and subsequently to soil
samples. The marginal predictive CDFs are demonstrated in Figure 5.16 along with the
empirical distribution of the observed site investigation data in Table 5.7. Note that the
legend of Figure 5.16 refers to the multivariate distribution from which the X samples were
simulated, except from the site ECDF (directly from the site observations). Comments on
marginal distributions of Figure 5.16:
вҖў When no site observations are available, the hybrid and predictive Bayesian CDFs are
similar, and the site specific (predictive) CDF is flat. In these cases, the regional PDF
governs the behavior of the soil property, and predictions at the site can be made based
on these distributions. This is evident in the first two subplots of Figure 5.16
вҖў For soil properties for which the regional data indicated low statistical uncertainty,
e.g. for ПҒd, or relatively low for (q/p)200 (see Table 4.5), it can be seen that the tree
distributions are close to each other, and they are also close to the empirical CDF of the
site data.
вҖў In most cases, the site-specific predictive CDF tends to follow closely the site ECDF.
For parameters with a large number of observations in the regional database e.g.
LL, PI, ic, w, the marginal CDFs constructed from f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o) appears to be close
to the CDFs constructed from the unconditional (regional prior) f (x|Вөg, Cg. In these
cases, f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o) carries more information about the regional data due to the large
number of different sites, and the relatively low amount of site observations compared
to those included in the regional database.
The univariate information on the soil parameters at the site may be low, but is consid-
erably larger than the pairwise information on which correlations depend, as discussed
previously. With regard to the marginal CDFs, the hybrid distribution appears to
describe better the site observations. In addition, it keeps a balance between regional
and site-specific CDF. When the uncertainty is high in the site data, e.g. for c, E50,200, or
relatively high e.g. П•, the hybrid CDF retains valuable information from the regional
data, deviates from the site-specific CDF, but in lesser degree than the regional CDF. In
the consistency, and Atterberg limits parameters, there is a noticeable deviation between
the hybrid and the rest marginal CDFs, where the hybrid CDF is much closer to the site
data ECDF. For instance, the hybrid and site ECDF of consistency index ic shows that
the propertyвҖҷs values at the site, fall in the interval [0.8 вҲ’1.3], which can be an indication
120
==PAGE:126==
5 Results
of stiff to very stiff clayey soils at the site (e.g. (Das, 2015) pp. 97). Considering the large
number of observations in the regional database for these parameters, and the relatively
low number of available observations at the site (see Table 5.7), more measurements in
the future can be made for validation of the hybrid and Bayesian marginal CDFs results.
However, it is evident in Figure 5.16, that the performance of both methods, is more
than satisfying when it comes to describing limited site observations, and characterize
the uncertainty in the soil parameters at the site level.
Descriptive statistics were calculated from the simulated soil samples, and these are
provided in Table 5.10. These can be compared to the descriptive statistics computed from
the soil data in the regional database, given in table 4.5. The coefficient of variation was
substantially reduced in index properties. Reduction in the the COV of the design parameter
friction angle, was achieved as well with the Bayesian approach, although this was not high.
The COV of cohesion c and YoungвҖҷs modulus E50,200 remained at high levels, indicating high
uncertainty for these parameters. On the other hand, the Bayesian approach appeared to
significantly reduce the COV of the (q/p)200 ratio, which was evident in the correlation plots
of this parameter (see Appendix Figure A.8). In addition, this reduction can provide more
reliable estimates on the friction angle values, since one can utilize the correlation plot of
((q/p)200, П•), and the narrow confidence intervals. Published COV values (e.g. see Table 3.1),
indicate that the marginal statistics in the present study, are within the bounds of general
soil categories. The effective cohesion parameter presents higher CoV than the suggested
upper bound of the statistic range in Table 3.1). A comparison between the Bayesian methodвҖҷs
marginals statistics in Table 5.10, and the CoVs in the descriptive statistics of the regional
database, Table 4.5, shows high reduction in the CoV of the index properties, while lower, but
considerable reduction is achieved for the YoungвҖҷs modulus E50,200.

measurements on the soil proportions and SkemptonвҖҷs activity (A) are completely missing. It
is reasonable that predictions for these parameters will depend on the regional data.

Simulation from the regional unconditional multivariate distribution
Given the covariance matrix the unconditional regional PDF can be constructed. It is con-
sidered that the variables (X1, ..., X8) are jointly normal distributed, with zero mean vector
and unit standard deviations. Unconditional samples can be generated from the distribution,
in a similar way as discussed in section 5.2.1. The simulation results on the correlations
between the design parameter undrained shear strength and su,uc and the rest soil parameters
in the dataset are provided in Figure 5.18 Simulated samples from the unconditional JPDF
are plotted (grey circles) along with the pairwise data-points in the regional database, and
with the computed moving mean, and Confidence Intervals. The CIвҖҷs correspond to the
(2.5%, 97.5%) percentiles, i.e. 95% CIs. The illustrated 95% CIs were computed from a large
number n = 2 В· 106 samples. 2000 of them are shown in each subplot of Figure 5.18. It
125
==PAGE:131==
5 Results
is evident that the pairwise observations between su,uc and the considered soil properties,
are more than those of the triaxial testing parameter П• shown in Figure 5.10. Noticeable
correlations can be observed between (w, su,uc), (LI, su,uc), and (ПҒd, su,uc), while the undrained
shear strength appears to be weakly correlated with the soil proportions.
5.3.2 The quasi-Site-specific model based on Bayesian updating with conjugate
priors and Gibbs Sampling
The Gibbs Sampler was utilized to obtain posterior samples of the parameters of the regional
database Вөdb, Cdb. The GS samples were then utilized, and based on the total probability
theorem, the predictive regional distribution f (x|Xdb,o) which characterizes the uncertainty
of the considered variables, given the observed data in the regional database, was derived
as a mixture of multivariate normal PDFs. The predictive distribution was then conditioned
on the limited site observations of Table 5.11, to derive the quasi-site specific distribution
f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o) with Bayesian updating.
The hybridization approach was tested, as well. The Gibbs sampler was used to obtain
posterior samples of Вөs, Cs, based on the sparse site data of Table 5.11. The site specific
predictive PDF was constructed as a mixture of multivariate normal PDFs, using the generated
samples. The quasi-site specific model was derived from the product of the unconditional
regional PDF f (0, Cg), where Cg) from bootstrap samples given in Table 5.13, and the site
specific predictive PDF f (x|Xs,o).
For the Gibbs Sampler, T = 10000 cycles were considered and tb = 1000 samples were burnt
out after visual inspection. Figure illustrates a histogram of posterior regional correlation
coefficient samples, extracted from each cycle of the Gibbs Sampling, along with i) the
correlation coefficient between (Xi, Xj) from the original regional data and, ii) the average
value of the generated samples with the bootstrap technique. Similar remarks can be made on
the correlation coefficients of Figure 5.19, as those of Figure 5.11, and possible weakness of the
bootstrap method to capture possible high correlation coefficients, can be again noticed. The
peak of the GS coefficient histogram of ПҒ3,8, shows a moderate degree of negative correlation
between undrained shear strength and water content, while in the subplot of ПҒ7,8, a moderate
degree of positive correlation between dry density and undrained shear strength is indicated.
The simulation results from the conditional on the site data predictive PDF, obtained
with the Bayesian method are illustrated in Figure 5.20. The simulation results from the
Hybridization approach are demonstrated in Figure 5.21. Simulation results for the correlation
between su,uc, and w, PL and LI are provided in these figures. Since no site observations were
available for the soil proportions and SkemptonвҖҷs activity, the simulation results that involve
these variables were very similar to those of the unconditional distribution, given in Figure
5.18. The regional predictive distribution approaches the unconditional distribution due to
the relative large number of observations in this case. In addition the marginal distributions
of these variables which are provided later in this section, indicated similar behavior. With
regard to the correlation of su,uc with the dry density ПҒd, similar type of information can be
produced from the correlation plot between (w, su,uc), hence the former is not illustrated here.
Comments on the simulation results of Figures 5.20, 5.21:
126
==PAGE:132==
5 Results
вҖў In comparison to Figure 5.18, the scatter of simulated samples from both methods was
reduced. The reduction in the scatter, and consequently the uncertainty in the bivariate
correlations, was more evident in the simulation results of the Hybrid multivariate PDF.
This remark can be explained by
- the larger number of univariate data-points for the design parameter su,uc, and
the increased amount of pairwise information in the regional regional dataset UC,
between the strength parameter and the soil properties (see Tables 4.5, 4.8)
- The conditional Bayesian predictive PDF f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o) is expected to approach
the regional predictive PDF f (x|Xdb,o), which in turn approaches the unconditional
PDF due to the larger number of observations (compared to the triaxial dataset),
and the large uncertainty in su,uc at the site
вҖў The reduction in the uncertainty of su,uc was not large, and this was only observed in
the simulation results of the Hybrid PDF. It can be noticed in both Figures 5.20, 5.21,
that the scatter indicates a large range over which possible new observations of the
parameter are likely to fall. Large uncertainty is evident at the site data, with two su,uc
observations having values over 540 (kPa), that can be related to very stiff soil. The
statistical uncertainty in the site-specific predictive PDF is also high due to limited
amount of observations. The high uncertainty in su,uc evident in the marginal CDFs
which are presented later in this section.
вҖў Weak negative correlation between (w, su,uc) and (LI, su,uc), appear to apply at the site,
captured at specific regions of w and LI.
A comparison between the simulation results on the bivariate correlations is visualized in
Figure 5.22. Updated CIs are provided based on the simulated samples from the Bayesian
f (x|Xdb,o, Xs,o). The CIs are quite large, they include the site data, and they reflect high
uncertainty at the soil layer of the site.
Similar remarks can be made when looking at the KDE approximation of the bivariate PDFs
from the simulated data, demonstrated in Figure 5.23. Note that only the approximations
obtained from the hybrid samples are provided, since they appeared to be more reliable
than the the respective approximations with the Bayesian simulated samples. In the latter
approximations, the gaussian KDE assigned density at su,uc values very close to zero. A large
number of n = 15000 simulated samples was used to approximate the bivariate distributions
of (w, su,uc), (PL, su,uc) and (LI, su,uc). The bandwidths were computed with the "silverman"
rule of thumb, and validated with "leave-one-out" cross validation. It is reminded that the
numerical values of the presented approximations of the bivariate PDFs, should be used with
caution, while for reliability purposes, the simulated samples should be utilized instead.

5.3.3 Marginal distributions

is flat for three parameters since no observations were available at the site, and for these

the SkemptonвҖҷs activity. The Hybrid CDF appears to describe better the site data.

6 Summary and conclusion
6.1 Thesis contribution
This study adopts fundamental and state-of-the-art methods, to investigate how a geotechnical
engineer nowadays can efficiently handle and analyze Big Indirect Data (BID) in the form
of soil databases, and how one can benefit from the application of probabilistic analysis in
geotechnical practice. This study covers many aspects of site investigation in geotechnical
analysis. Readily available and pre-processed datasets under ideal conditions, are very far
from reality in geotechnical applications. The present study attempts to operate under real-life
conditions, starting from handling raw data, computing meaningful soil parameters to include
in the analysis, and building a database that allows application of probabilistic methods. The
constructed regional database contains data under MUSIC (Multivariate Unique Sparse and
potentially Corrupted) characteristics, collected from a large number of site investigation
programs, over a 12-year period. The database was compiled from measurements and derived
geotechnical properties obtained specifically from results of laboratory tests such as: sieve
analysis, plasticity, unconfined compression and triaxial tests. The present study adds to
the development of a relational database containing several testsвҖҷ results, using open-source
platforms and employing SQL and Python programming languages. The assumptions made
to derive the database were noted down in detail, such as expert engineering judgement can
be applied in almost every step of the procedure.
The Bayesian framework was adopted to select marginal distributions that better describe the
M-C shear strength parametersвҖҷ data in the regional database, to compute reliable statistics,
and to account for the uncertainty in the distribution parameters. The best fit marginals were
further utilized for coupling different dependent soil parameters, to construct a multivariate
distribution. The model is based on the joint normality assumption of transformed to the
standard space random variables, that allows making use of the Gaussian copula framework.
The constructed model efficiently incorporates prior information on a regional scale and can
serve as prior distribution in the Bayesian updating process. The approach provides a means
to simulate dependent samples from a multivariate distribution. Confidence intervals were
computed representative for bivariate correlations at a regional scale. The simulation results
showed that the statistical dependency structure between triaxial testing parameters and
several index properties, can be reasonably captured with the Gaussian copula assumption.
The main part of the study was focused on constructing a site-specific multivariate model,
able to characterize the statistical uncertainty in cross-correlations at the site level. The
approach followed the proposed methods of (Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2014; Ching, K. K.
Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020; Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019), that provide a powerful means for
exploiting BID under MUSIC constraints. Two models were constructed based on the same
134
==PAGE:140==
6 Summary and conclusion
principles: i) a model that involves triaxial testing parameters such as friction angle, cohesion
and YoungвҖҷs modulus, with sieve analysis results and Atterberg limits and, ii) a model that
includes the undrained shear strength from unconfined compression tests and similar index
properties. The model is based solely on data, i.e it is a Machine Learning method, that
operates under the Bayesian framework. The choice of specific prior PDFs for constructing
the regional model, and the site-specific distribution from limited and sparse site data, in
conjunction with advanced sampling techniques, allowed the hybridization of the two models
under a modified Bayesian framework. The resulting multivariate distribution can be viewed
as a quasi-site-specific model which can be used for enhanced site-specific predictions. The
results on the cross-correlations between soil parameters of interest, as well as the derived
marginal distributions of several soil parameters in the dataset, were compared to those of
Bayesian Analysis with conjugate priors and Gibbs Sampling.
The results showed that the derived quasi-site-specific models are able to capture, localise,
and reduce the statistical uncertainty in the cross-correlations, in cases where i) correlations
among soil propertiesвҖҷ data do indeed exist in some degree in the regional data, and ii) a
reasonable number of pairwise observations are available in regional and site data. Both
methods reduce the uncertainty in bivariate correlations in comparison to the unconditional
regional model. Simulation results from the conditional on the site data Bayesian distribution
revealed stronger correlations between friction angle and the soil index properties. On the
other hand, the updated marginal distributions indicated that the Hybridization approach
can better describe the limited sparse site data in the triaxial related dataset, while keeping
a balance between regional and site-specific distributions, when the uncertainty at the site
is high. Results related to the YoungвҖҷs modulus indicated large uncertainty in the bivariate
correlations associated with this parameter and more observations are needed for more
reliable predictions.
With regard to the simulation results on correlations between the undrained shear strength,
both methods indicated large uncertainty in this design parameter, which can be explained
by i) the large uncertainty in the regional data (вүҲ67% CoV), ii) the low number of site
observations and the large uncertainty at the site. Nevertheless, the Bayesian approach
reduces the uncertainty in the CoV of soil index properties and the undrained shear strength,
while the hybridization method appeared to reduce the scatter in the simulated samples of
the bivariate correlations.
Despite that the models do not explicitly characterize the spatial correlation between soil
properties, it allowed the derivation of site-specific distributions for soil parameters, and
updated marginal statistics, representative of the propertiesвҖҷ behavior within the bounds of
an assumed statistically homogenous site soil layer. The computed CoVs indicated that most
coefficients of variation (CoVs), fall within recommended ranges of published guidelines for
comparable soil types.
135
==PAGE:141==
6 Summary and conclusion
6.2 Future improvements
вҖў Expert knowledge is needed for checking the validity of the compiled soil database
for general use. Engineering judgement is necessary for reality checks to the modelsвҖҷ
results. The number of pairwise observations in the triaxial testing parametersвҖҷ dataset
was low for a regional database. More pairwise measurements can allow more reliable
characterization of the correlation behaviors.
вҖў Future attempts on the extension of the compiled regional database, should consider to
providing a better connection with the available geologic models in the region. Ground-
water levels and conditions, soil layering, important parameters for the analysis such as
overburden stresses, over-consolidation ratio, sensitivity, etc., should be incorporated.
вҖў The construction of the multivariate models was based on the degree of correlation
between the soil parameters, using sample coefficients computed from regional data.
Sensitivity analysis should be carried out for the modelsвҖҷ inputs, and this can be an
important add-in in future studies.
вҖў The bootstrap technique appeared to provide low correlation coefficients. The method
could be improved or replaced in future applications.
вҖў The measurement error was not explicitly considered in this study. Measurement errors
can be incorporated as separate random variables in the analysis. Advanced outlier
detection algorithms can be also used to remove bad quality data associated with
laboratory measurements and derived properties. Machine Learning methods have
proved their potential in extracting hidden features of large multivariate datasets, and
such techniques should be tested for future improvements.
вҖў The examined local site included limited and very sparse data collection. Data from
in-situ tests can be utilized to strengthen the support of the database, allowing the
use of geotechnical transformation models, and provide a better basis for applying the
proposed Bayesian methods.
вҖў The present study focused on the cross-correlations between soil parameters. The
multivariate models are able to predict values of a soil parameter at a specific depth in
specific locations of a soil layer at the site. The prediction results can be checked with
actual measured values from in-situ and laboratory tests.
вҖў The simulation results can be used to construct regional and local transformation models,
able to replace global correlations, that often include a large amount of transformation
uncertainty.
вҖў This study focused on fine grained soils, with clay as the main type. Sands can be also
considered in future applications. The compiled databases contain soil classification
labels, which can be used to analyze data belonging to smaller, but more specific soil
groups.
136
==PAGE:142==
6 Summary and conclusion
вҖў The modeling of the spatial correlation of soil properties with random fields, should be
explicitly considered in future studies.
вҖў Worked examples on actual reliability problems are needed to check the performance of
the proposed methods and to allow comparison with current practices.
6.3 Outlook
Several methods were utilized in this study to get a closer insight into laboratory testsвҖҷ related
soil properties in a regional scale. Compiling a regional database, is not straightforward,
since one has to deal with the incomplete feature of the data, and this becomes ever more
complex when one wishes to group observations with respect to some similar soil conditions.
The regional probability distributions of soil properties can reflect prior knowledge on
their statistical uncertainty and inherent variability. The adopted Gaussian copula based
method for constructing joint probability distribution models can be used to examine the
inter-dependency of several geotechnical properties. The approach can be improved in future
studies by making use of more advanced copula functions.
The derived quasi-site-specific models, with the application of the proposed methods of
(Ching and K. K. Phoon, 2019; Ching, K. K. Phoon, Khan, et al., 2020), provide a strong
basis for characterizing the site-specific uncertainty in correlations between soil properties.
The database should be extended to include more pairwise observations. An extension of
the proposed method to account for spatial-cross-correlations can be the cornerstone towards
a full Data-driven probabilistic characterization framework in the future. The advantages
of such an approach for complementing and supporting sound engineering judgement and
expert knowledge in design decisions, are more than obvious.
